المبتدأ الذي هو صدرُ الكلام من غير ضرورة وقرينة واضحة. وأما ثانياً : فلحذف الضمير في الجملة التي وقعت صفة. وأما ثالثاً : فلأن استثناء قول أبي يوسف له على هذا لغو، فإنّ عنده لا توجد صورة يكون الطهر الناقص فيها فاصلاً، فإنّه يجعلُ الطهر الذي هو أقل من خمسة عشر يوماً مطلقاً غير فاصل كان أقل من ثلاثة أو أكثر فأي حاجة إلى استثنائه بعد توصيف الصورة بما وصفها به، إلا أن يقال : هو لمجرد التوضيح، ودفع إيهام دخول قوله في قوله في هذه الأقوال. ومنهم من قال : إن قوله «يكون صفة للصورة، والعائد محذوف، وقوله: «كلّ صورة» بالإضافة ظرف لقوله : «فإن كان أحد الدمين ...الخ، والفاء في قوله : «فإن كان زائدة، فصار التقدير هكذا إذا عرفت ما ذكرنا فإن كان أحد الدمين نصاباً في كلّ صورة يكون فيها الطهر الناقص فاصلاً كان ذلك الدم حيضاً... الخ. ولا يخفى ما فيه أيضاً من التكلّف من التزام زيادة الفاء، والتزام حذف الضمير، والتزام تقديم ما في حيز الشرط على الشرط من غير ضرورة. ومنهم من قال : إنّ «كل صورة بالإضافة مبتدأ ، وجملة «يكون الطهر» ..الخ، خبره ، واللام الداخلة على الطهر الناقص للعهد، والمراد به الذي زاد على ثلاثة ونقص
The book of purification / 013. According to Abu Sahl: the first six of them. According to Al-Hasan: the last four from forty-five, and what is other than that is istihadah. And the other pure (period) predominant at its two ends, if one suffices with the true blood that is overcome, if the legal blood is considered included in it, and the remaining days are istihadah. [1] His statement: the first six of them; i.e., from the ten which are after a pure period of seven, which are entirely hayd according to Muhammad; because he considers equality of the pure period or its minimality with respect to the two true bleedings surrounding it, and does not consider the legal blood. And this condition exists in the first six of the two (sets) in which she saw two days of blood and three of purity, then one day of blood, so this amount is hayd, and the days before these six and after them are all istihadah. [2] His statement: the last four; i.e., from forty-five days, which have blood at the beginning and blood at the end, with two days of purity in between; for the intervening pure period is less than three days, so it is hayd according to him, excluding the remaining days before these four, because their condition is lacking; for the intervening pure periods are more than three days, so they are separating; i.e., they are not hayd. [3] His statement: and what is other than that; i.e., what is other than what we mentioned of the days that are hayd according to any of the previous madhhabs, it is istihadah according to that madhhab, due to the lack of the condition. And this is an explicit statement that the conditions stipulated by the proponents of the mentioned opinions, except the statement of Abu Yusuf, are only for the intervening pure period to be hayd, not for the pure period to be considered continuous blood absolutely. So all those days are considered continuous blood according to all, except that hayd from them is the amount that meets the conditions according to each opinion, and the rest is istihadah. And by this the error of what the author of 'al-Ta'liq al-Kamil' understood is manifest, that the conditions are conditions for the pure period to be continuous blood, and his error was clarified by the respected father, may Allah admit him to the abode of peace, in the sequel of the treatise 'al-Ta'liq al-Fasil'. So in every case, the deficient pure period is a separator in these opinions except the statement of Abu Yusuf. [1] His statement: 'So in every case...' etc.; the commentators differed on the construction of this sentence and what is connected to it and the explanation of its meaning. Some said: 'kullu suratin' in construct is the predicate of a deleted subject that is delayed, and his statement 'yakuunu al-naaqisu faasilan' is an adjective for 'surah', and the pronoun referring back to the described is deleted, and his statement 'fa-in kaana' is an explanation of the deleted subject. So the estimated phrase is: when you know what we have mentioned, then in every case in which the deficient pure period is a separator, it is a detail: that if one of the two surrounding bleedings is a nisab (i.e., three to ten days), it is hayd... etc. And the difficulty in this is not hidden from the trained: first, because of the necessity of deleting the subject which is the beginning of the speech without necessity and clear context; second, because of deleting the pronoun in the sentence that functions as an adjective; third, because excluding the statement of Abu Yusuf on this is meaningless, since according to him there is no case where the deficient pure period is a separator, for he considers the pure period that is less than fifteen days absolutely as not separating, whether it is less than three or more, so what need is there to exclude him after describing the case as described, unless it is said that it is merely for clarification and to remove the illusion that his statement enters into these opinions. And some said: his statement 'yakuunu' is an adjective for 'surah', and the pronoun is deleted, and his statement 'kullu suratin' in construct is a circumstantial adverb for his statement 'fa-in kaana ahadu al-damayni...', and the fa in 'fa-in kaana' is extra. So the estimated phrase is: when you know what we have mentioned, then if one of the two bleedings is a nisab in every case in which the deficient pure period is a separator, that blood is hayd... etc. And the difficulty in this also is not hidden, involving the assumption of an extra fa, the deletion of the pronoun, and placing what is in the scope of the condition before the condition without necessity. And some said: 'kullu suratin' in construct is a subject, and the clause 'yakuunu al-tahru'... etc. is its predicate, and the lam entering 'al-tahr al-naaqis' is for that which has been mentioned, and what is meant is what exceeds three and falls short (of fifteen).
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on determining which days of bleeding constitute menstruation (hayd) when a woman experiences a pattern of bleeding and purity over a period. The commentary expands on a matn that lists different opinions. The main conditions discussed are: the intervening pure period (tahr) between two bleedings must be less than three days for the bleeding days to be considered continuous hayd; otherwise, the pure period separates the bleedings, and only the bleeding that meets the minimum (nisab, 3-10 days) is hayd. The first part states views: 'Abu Sahl' (likely Abu Hanifa) says the first six days; Al-Hasan says the last four days from a forty-five-day cycle; what remains is istihadah. The commentator explains that according to Imam Muhammad, the first six days from a ten-day period after a seven-day pure period are hayd because the intervening pure periods (two days here) are less than three. For the last four days in a forty-five-day cycle, the intervening pure period is two days, also less than three, so those four days are hayd. For other days, the intervening pure periods are longer than three days, so they separate. The passage then clarifies that all these days are considered continuous blood in the sense of connecting, but only the portion meeting the conditions is hayd. The author criticizes a misunderstanding by the author of 'al-Ta'liq al-Kamil' who thought the conditions affect the legal continuity of blood. The final part of the passage delves into the grammatical parsing of a sentence from the matn: 'fafi kulli suratin yakuunu al-tahr al-naqis fasilan' (so in every case, the deficient pure period is a separator). Several interpretations are discussed, each with difficulties. The preferred interpretation is that 'kullu suratin' is the subject, the clause is the predicate, and 'al-tahr al-naqis' refers to a pure period exceeding three days but less than the minimum (15? or less than the full purity period). This grammatical discussion is important for understanding the scope of the rule. Throughout, the text emphasizes that the condition for hayd is that the intervening purity is less than three days, and that this is agreed upon except for Abu Yusuf's view, which makes any purity less than 15 days as not separating.

Key Terms
الطهر الناقص (al-tahr al-naqis): A pure period (tahr) that is less than three days or, in some interpretations, more than three but less than fifteen days; in this text, it refers to purity that does not separate the two bleedings according to the condition.
النصاب (al-nisab): The minimum duration of hayd, which is three days (and the maximum is ten days).
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Bleeding that is not menstrual but from a vein; the woman in istihadah performs wudu for each prayer and prays despite bleeding.
الدم الحقيقي (al-dam al-haqiqi): Actual blood that appears, as opposed to 'legal blood' (al-dam al-hukmi) which is the legal presumption of blood due to continuity.
الدم الحكمي (al-dam al-hukmi): Legal blood; the ruling that blood is considered present for legal purposes even if not actually seen, e.g., during a pure period that is deemed part of continuous bleeding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, what is the condition for an intervening pure period (tahr) to not break the connection between two bleedings?
A) It must be less than 15 days.
B) It must be less than 3 days.
C) It must be exactly 7 days.
D) It must be more than 3 days.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school unanimously agrees that an intervening pure period of three days or more separates two bleedings, but differs on whether a shorter purity also separates; the majority (mufta bihi) says it does not.

Taharah — Purification

The Deficient Pure Period as a Separator: Hanafi Ruling and Grammatical Support
Linguistic parsing supporting a fiqh ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the short pure period (tuhr nāqiṣ) as a separator and identify the intra-madhhab dispute with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A pure period between 3 and 15 days separates two menstrual cycles according to most Hanafis, but Abu Yusuf disagrees.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: whether a short period of purity between two blood flows acts as a separator. The source passage reviews several grammatical interpretations of a sentence from al-Wiqayah, each attempting to preserve the majority view. While the discussion is linguistic, the legal consequence is clear: the deficient pure period (tuhr nāqiṣ) is a separator, except according to Imam Abu Yusuf. We will extract the ruling and understand its practical application.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify what the Hanafi school says about the deficient pure period: it is a separator (fāṣil) between two menstrual bleeding episodes, provided it lasts more than 3 days but less than 15.
This is the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Identify the intra-madhhab dispute
Note that Abu Yusuf holds a different opinion: he considers any pure period under 15 days as non-separating, regardless of its length.
This is the exception mentioned in the sentence.
3
Understand the grammatical challenge
The commentators try to parse the sentence 'في كل صورة يكون الظهر الناقص فاصلاً في هذه الأقوال سوى قول أبي يوسف' so that the exception is correctly interpreted: the deficient pure period is a separator in all statements except Abu Yusuf's.
The tanwin on 'kull' is key: it substitutes for a deleted genitive word (taqdīr), meaning 'in every estimation'.
4
Trace the practical consequence
The ruling affects how a woman determines her menstrual cycle: if a purity period of 4-14 days occurs between two blood flows, the Hanafi majority consider it a separator, meaning two separate menstrual periods; Abu Yusuf would consider it one continuous menstruation.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text

ففي كل صورة يكون الظهر الناقص فاصلاً في هذه الأقوال سوى قول أبي يوسف له

قال: ففي كل صورة... الخ؛ اختلف الناظرون في تركيب هذه الجملة وما يتصل بها وبيان معناها:
فمنهم من قال: إن كل صورة بالإضافة خبر مبتدأ محذوف مؤخراً، وقوله: يكون الناقص فاصلاً صفة لقوله: صورة، والضمير العائد إلى الموصوف محذوف، وقوله: «فإن كان بيان للمبتدأ المحذوف، فصار التقدير إذا عرفت ما ذكرنا، ففي كلِّ صورة يكون الطهر الناقص فيها فاصلاً تفصيل؛ وهو أنه إن كان أحد الدمين المحيطين به نصاباً؛ أي ثلاثة أيام إلى عشرة كان حيضاً... الخ.
ولا يخفى على المتدرب ما فيه من التكلف:
أما أولاً: فللزوم حذف المبتدأ الذي هو صدرُ الكلام من غير ضرورة وقرينة واضحة.
وأما ثانياً: فلحذف الضمير في الجملة التي وقعت صفة.
وأما ثالثاً: فلأن استثناء قول أبي يوسف له على هذا لغو، فإنّ عنده لا توجد صورة يكون الطهر الناقص فيها فاصلاً، فإنّه يجعلُ الطهر الذي هو أقل من خمسة عشر يوماً مطلقاً غير فاصل كان أقل من ثلاثة أو أكثر فأي حاجة إلى استثنائه بعد توصيف الصورة بما وصفها به، إلا أن يقال: هو لمجرد التوضيح، ودفع إيهام دخول قوله في قوله في هذه الأقوال.
ومنهم من قال: إن قوله «يكون صفة للصورة، والعائد محذوف، وقوله: «كلّ صورة» بالإضافة ظرف لقوله: «فإن كان أحد الدمين... الخ، والفاء في قوله: «فإن كان زائدة، فصار التقدير هكذا إذا عرفت ما ذكرنا فإن كان أحد الدمين نصاباً في كلّ صورة يكون فيها الطهر الناقص فاصلاً كان ذلك الدم حيضاً... الخ.
ولا يخفى ما فيه أيضاً من التكلّف من التزام زيادة الفاء، والتزام حذف الضمير، والتزام تقديم ما في حيز الشرط على الشرط من غير ضرورة.
ومنهم من قال: إنّ «كل صورة بالإضافة مبتدأ، وجملة «يكون الطهر» ... الخ خبره، واللام الداخلة على الطهر الناقص للعهد، والمراد به الذي زاد على ثلاثة ونقص من خمسة عشر، فالمعنى في كل صورة ذلك الطهر الناقص فاصل في جميع الأقوال، إلا قول أبي يوسف له فإنه ليس بفاصل عنده مطلقاً. وفيه أيضاً ما لا يخفى للزوم إهمال قوله: في كل صورة.
ومنهم من قال: الفعل أعني يكون منزلاً منزلة المصدر، فهو مبتدأ، وقوله: «في هذه الأقوال خبر والمراد بالناقص هو المعهود، فالمعنى كون الطهر الناقص وهو ما كان أكثر من ثلاثة فاصلاً ثابت في جميع الأقوال، سوى قول أبي يوسف له، فإنّه ليس بفاصل عنده، ولا يخفى ما فيه من السخافة للزوم لغوية قوله: «كل صورة»، ولزوم حمل اللام على العهد.
ومنهم من قال: إن تنوين «كل» عوض عن المضاف إليه، وهو لفظ: «تقدير»، وقوله: «صورة» مع صفتها، وهي جملة يكون الطهر» مبتدأ خبره قوله «في هذه الأقوال، فالمعنى أنه في كل تقدير الصورة التي يكون الطهر الناقص فيها فاصلاً موجودة في هذه الأقوال سوى قول أبي يوسف .
وفيه أيضاً من السخافة ما لا يخفى للزوم لغوية قوله: كل تقدير، ولزوم حذف لفظ «تقدير» بدون قرينة، ولزوم حذف العائد في الجملة.
ومنهم من قال: إن إحدى الفائين أعني فاء قوله: «ففي كل صورة»، وفاء قوله: «فإن كان زائدة، وقوله: «صورة» بالرفع مبتدأ، والجملة التالية صفة له، وخبره قوله: «في هذه الأقوال». يعني فصورة يكون الطهر الناقص فيها فاصلاً موجودة في هذه الأقوال سوى قول أبي يوسف له، فإنّ تلك الصورة غير متحققة فيها لعدم كون الطهر الناقص فاصلا عنده مطلقاً، وقوله: «فإن كان... الخ، تفصيل لتلك الصورة.
وفيه من التكلّف والتعسف ما لا يخفى.
والذي اختاره الوالد العلام ادخله الله دار السلام تبعاً لأستاذه وأب أم أبيه، وهو مولانا محمد ظهور الله له: إن تنوين «كل» عوض عن المضاف إليه، وقوله: «في هذه الأقوال بدل من قوله: «في كلّ»، بإعادة الجار، وقوله: «صورة» مع صفتها، وهي به مع الضمير المحذوف خبر لقوله: «كل».
الجملة المتصلة.
In every situation the deficient pure period is a separator in these statements except the statement of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him. He said: 'In every situation...' etc.; the commentators disagreed on the construction of this sentence and what is connected to it and clarifying its meaning. Some of them said: 'In every situation' as a possessive is the predicate of a deleted delayed subject, and his statement 'the deficient is a separator' is an adjective for his word 'situation', and the pronoun referring to the described is deleted, and his statement 'for if' is an explanation of the deleted subject, so the estimation becomes: if you know what we mentioned, then in every situation in which the deficient pure period is a separator, a detailed explanation; and it is that if one of the two surrounding bloods is a full period, i.e., three days to ten, it is menstruation... etc. And the difficulty in it is not hidden from the trainee: First, because of the necessity of deleting the subject which is the beginning of speech without necessity and clear context. Second, because of deleting the pronoun in the sentence that is an adjective. Third, because the exclusion of Abu Yusuf's statement is meaningless in this, for in his opinion there is no situation in which the deficient pure period is a separator, because he makes the pure period that is less than fifteen days absolutely not a separator, whether it is less than three or more. So what need is there to exclude him after describing the situation as described? Unless it is said: it is merely for clarification and to repel the illusion that his statement enters into 'in these statements'. And some of them said: his statement 'is' is an adjective for the situation, and the pronoun is deleted, and his statement 'every situation' as possessive is an adverb for his statement 'for if one of the two bloods...' etc., and the 'fa' in his statement 'for if' is redundant, so the estimation is: if you know what we mentioned, then if one of the two bloods is a full period in every situation in which the deficient pure period is a separator, then that blood is menstruation... etc. And what is in it of difficulty is not hidden, from the commitment of a redundant 'fa', commitment of deleting the pronoun, and commitment of placing what is in the condition before the condition without necessity. And some of them said: 'every situation' as possessive is a subject, and the sentence 'is the pure period' ... etc. is its predicate, and the 'lam' entering on 'the deficient pure period' is for the known, and what is meant is that which exceeded three and was less than fifteen. So the meaning: in every situation that deficient pure period is a separator in all statements, except the statement of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, for it is not a separator according to him absolutely. And in it also is what is not hidden, due to the necessity of ignoring his statement 'in every situation'. And some of them said: the verb, namely 'is', is treated as a verbal noun, so it is a subject, and his statement 'in these statements' is a predicate, and what is meant by 'the deficient' is the known one. So the meaning: the existence of the deficient pure period, which is more than three, as a separator is established in all statements, except the statement of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, for it is not a separator according to him. And the nonsense in it is not hidden, due to the necessity of the uselessness of his statement 'every situation', and the necessity of interpreting the 'lam' as for the known. And some of them said: the tanwin of 'every' is a substitute for the genitive, which is the word 'estimation', and his statement 'situation' with its description, which is the sentence 'the pure period is', is a subject whose predicate is his statement 'in these statements'. So the meaning: in every estimation of the situation in which the deficient pure period is a separator exists in these statements except the statement of Abu Yusuf. And in it also is nonsense that is not hidden, due to the necessity of the uselessness of his word 'every estimation', the necessity of deleting the word 'estimation' without context, and the necessity of deleting the pronoun in the sentence. And some of them said: one of the two 'fa's, namely the 'fa' of his statement 'so in every situation' and the 'fa' of his statement 'for if' are redundant, and his word 'situation' in the nominative is a subject, the following sentence is its adjective, and its predicate is his statement 'in these statements'. That is, a situation in which the deficient pure period is a separator exists in these statements except the statement of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, for that situation is not realized in it due to the fact that the deficient pure period is not a separator according to him at all. And his statement 'for if ...' etc. is a detailed explanation of that situation. And in it is difficulty and strain that is not hidden. And what was chosen by my father, the scholar, may Allah enter him into the abode of peace, following his teacher and the father of his mother, namely our master Muhammad Zuhur Allah, may Allah have mercy on him: the tanwin of 'every' is a substitute for the genitive, and his statement 'in these statements' is a substitute for his statement 'in every situation' by repeating the preposition, and his statement 'situation' with its description, namely the sentence with the deleted pronoun, is the predicate of 'every'. The connected sentence.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is a detailed grammatical analysis of a sentence from the original text of al-Wiqayah. The sentence states: 'In every situation, the deficient pure period is a separator in these statements except the statement of Abu Yusuf.' The commentators propose multiple grammatical interpretations to clarify the meaning. The underlying ruling is about the legal status of a 'deficient pure period' (al-tuhr al-naqis) – a period of purity between two blood flows that is less than the minimum pure interval of 15 days but more than 3 days. According to the Hanafi school, such a period acts as a separator, meaning it interrupts the continuity of menstruation. However, Imam Abu Yusuf held that any pure period less than 15 days, regardless of whether it is less than 3 days or more, does not act as a separator. The various grammatical readings aim to accurately express that the short pure period (between 3 and 15 days) is a separator in most opinions except that of Abu Yusuf. The passage critically examines each proposed parsing, pointing out logical and grammatical weaknesses. The preferred interpretation, selected by the author's father, is that the tanwin on 'kull' (every) substitutes for the genitive 'taqdīr' (estimation), so the meaning is: 'In every estimation of the situation in which the deficient pure period is a separator, that situation exists in these statements except Abu Yusuf's statement.' This resolves the apparent contradictions in the earlier readings. The passage does not introduce a new ruling but clarifies the existing one through linguistic precision.

Key Terms
طهر ناقص (tuhr nāqiṣ): A period of purity between two blood flows that is less than the minimum pure interval (15 days) but more than 3 days; it serves as a separator according to most Hanafis.
فاصل (fāṣil): A separator that interrupts the continuity of blood flows; if a pure period is a fāṣil, it means the blood before and after are considered separate menstrual episodes.
تنوين عوض (tanwīn 'iwaḍ): The nunation on 'kull' that substitutes for a deleted genitive word (taqdīr 'estimation'), a grammatical feature.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a pure period (tuhr) that lasts 10 days between two blood flows?
A) It is not a separator; the blood before and after are one menstruation.
B) It is a separator, meaning two separate menstrual periods.
C) It is a separator only if it is exactly 15 days.
D) It is a separator only if the woman is not pregnant.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority holds that a deficient pure period (more than 3, less than 15 days) is a separator, with Abu Yusuf dissenting; the grammatical analysis confirms this meaning.

Incomplete Purity as a Separator in Hayd: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute with grammatical analysis on the wording of a sharh statement about partial purity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which incomplete purity (الطهر الناقص) serves as a separator (فاصل) according to different Hanafi scholars, and explain why Abu Yusuf rejects it absolutely.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school divides on whether purity over three days separates two menstrual periods; conditions matter except for Abu Yusuf.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the role of incomplete purity between two menstrual bleedings. While it is agreed that purity less than three days does not break continuity, longer but still incomplete purity (3–14 days) triggers disagreement. The passage presents the views of major Hanafi authorities and the conditions under which they treat it as a separator. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the school's methodology in cases of irregular bleeding.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify that Abu Hanifa, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and others hold that incomplete purity can be a separator (فاصل) under specific conditions, such as duration and context within ten days.
The conditions vary by scholar—e.g., Muhammad accepts 7 days of purity within 10 days as separator.
2
Identify the opposing view within the Hanafi school
Abu Yusuf holds that incomplete purity is never a separator; only complete purity (15 days) breaks continuity.
His view is absolute: no conditions can make incomplete purity a separator.
3
Follow the opponent's proof and basis
Abu Yusuf's reasoning is implicit: he likely considers any blood within the maximum period of hayd (10 days) as part of the same period unless a full pure interval (15 days) occurs. The text states he does not impose conditions.
No explicit dalil is quoted for his view.
4
Analyse the majority Hanafi response
The majority response criticises Abu Yusuf's view as lacking nuance. The text shows that for each scholar except Abu Yusuf, there exists a scenario where incomplete purity is a separator—e.g., 14 days of purity for all, 8 days for Ibn al-Mubarak, 7 days for Muhammad, 3 days for al-Hasan ibn Ziyad.
The response is based on practical experience (istihsan) and recognition that shorter pure intervals can be sufficient under specific circumstances.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Yusuf's view, a woman with intermittent bleeding would never have a clean break unless she experiences 15 consecutive days of purity, whereas the majority allow separation with shorter intervals, enabling her to pray and fast during those pure days.
This directly affects the woman's ability to resume acts of worship.

So the conclusion is that in each of these opinions except for the statement of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, there exists a form in which the incomplete purity is a separator, and it is that in which the considered conditions are missing, and this is not achievable according to the statement of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, because according to him non-separation is not conditional upon a condition.
And his contemporary, the author of al-Ta'liq al-Kamil, chose, following some of his teachers, what its summary is that 'كل صورة' with idafah is a subject and its predicate is his statement 'يكون', and the fa entering on his statement 'ففي' is for explanation.
And the intended meaning by 'كل صورة' is every form of the forms of the second section, which is when the incomplete purity is more than three days. And its clarification: That the commentator, may Allah have mercy on him, divided the intervening purity into two sections: The first: what was less than three, so it is not a separator by consensus.
:
And the second: what was three days or more, and he mentioned in it various forms and diverse opinions, then he interpreted them, then he said: 'ففي كل صورة...etc.' i.e., in every form of the second section, the incomplete purity is a separator in the permission of the two companions of these opinions, except for the statement of Abu Yusuf.
For example:
The first form: when the purity was fourteen days, so it is a separator according to them, based on their conditions that were mentioned, except for Abu Yusuf. And in the second form: which is when the purity is eight days surrounded by two bleedings within ten days, so it is a separator in the narration of Ibn al-Mubarak, may Allah have mercy on him, and others. And likewise in the third form: which is when the purity is seven days within ten days, so it is a separator according to Muhammad.
And likewise in the fourth form: which is when the intervening purity is three days, so it is a separator according to al-Hasan ibn Ziyad.
And as for the last form: which is when the purity is two days surrounded by two bleedings, it is outside the second section, so it is not a separator according to anyone, just as it is not a separator according to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, in all the mentioned forms, because he does not consider conditions. This, and in this place there is another detail whose place of expansion is al-Sa'ayah.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling of 'الطهر الناقص' (incomplete purity) as a separator (فاصل) between two menstrual bleedings. The text begins by presenting a sentence from the sharh (commentary) and then delves into various grammatical interpretations of its wording, specifically the phrase 'ففي كل صورة'. The core issue is whether the incomplete purity, defined as a period of purity exceeding three days, can break the continuity of two bleedings. The commentator lists multiple Hanafi opinions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani consider it a separator in certain conditions, while Abu Yusuf holds that it is never a separator. The text further details four specific forms from the second section (where the intervening purity is three days or more): (1) 14 days of purity – separator according to all except Abu Yusuf; (2) 8 days of purity surrounded by bleeding within 10 days – separator per Ibn al-Mubarak's narration; (3) 7 days of purity within 10 days – separator according to Muhammad; (4) 3 days of intervening purity – separator per al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. A fifth form (2 days) is excluded from the second section and is unanimously not a separator. The passage concludes that the relied-upon position (chosen by the author's father) is that in each opinion except Abu Yusuf's, there exists a scenario where incomplete purity is a separator, but Abu Yusuf rejects it absolutely because he does not impose conditions.

Key Terms
الطهر الناقص (al-tahru al-naqis): A period of purity between two menstrual bleedings that is less than the complete purity (15 days); specifically here, more than 3 days but less than 15.
فاصل (fasil): That which breaks the continuity between two bleedings, so that the later bleeding is considered a new menstrual period independent of the first.
الطهر المتخلل (al-tahru al-mutakhalil): The intervening purity between two bleeds; divided into less than 3 days (unanimously not a separator) and 3 days or more (disputed).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about the view of Abu Yusuf on incomplete purity?
A) He considers it a separator only if it is exactly 14 days.
B) He agrees with Muhammad that 7 days of purity can be a separator under conditions.
C) He does not consider any incomplete purity as a separator, regardless of conditions.
D) He only accepts incomplete purity as a separator when it is surrounded by bleeding within 10 days.

Key Takeaway
Incomplete purity (over 3 days) can serve as a separator between two menstrual periods according to most Hanafi scholars, but only under specific conditions; Abu Yusuf's absolute rejection is a minority view.

Module 11 · Lesson 421

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
The Deficient Purity Interval as Separator in Hayd: Abu Yusuf vs. The Majority
Analysis of Hanafi opinions on deficient purity between two blood flows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute on deficient purity intervals, identify the conditions under which the majority scholars allow separation, and apply the ruling to a concrete blood-purity-blood scenario.

Core Idea
A deficient purity can separate two blood flows only under specific conditions—Abu Yusuf rejects separation entirely.

In the Hanafi school, a full purity interval (al-tuhr al-kamil) of 15 days unequivocally separates two menstrual periods. But what if the purity is shorter, say 7 days between two blood flows? This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi divergence: the majority of Hanafi authorities (Imam Muhammad, Ibn al-Mubarak, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad) allow such a deficient purity (al-tuhr al-naqis) to act as a separator under certain conditions, while Abu Yusuf holds that it never separates. You will learn the exact conditions and how the dispute plays out in practical cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the question
The central question: can a purity interval shorter than 15 days (but at least 3 days) separate two blood flows, making each an independent hayd period? This matters for determining which days are hayd and which are istihadah.
2
State the position of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that any purity less than 15 days is not a separator. According to him, the two blood flows and the intervening purity are considered one continuous period if the total does not exceed the maximum hayd of 10 days.
This is the most restrictive view: deficient purity never separates.
3
State the position of the majority and their conditions
Muhammad al-Shaybani, Ibn al-Mubarak, and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad hold that deficient purity can separate, but only when specific conditions are met. The key condition is that the purity must be surrounded by two blood flows within the 10-day hayd window, and the purity must be of a certain length relative to the blood days.
Each scholar sets different conditions: e.g., Muhammad requires the purity to be equal to or less than the surrounding blood; Ibn al-Mubarak allows it if the purity is less than the blood; al-Hasan allows it with minimal purity of 3 days.
4
Apply the ruling to an example
Consider a woman who sees 3 days blood, 7 days purity, then 3 days blood. Under the majority's view, the first 3 days are hayd, the rest istihadah. Under Abu Yusuf's view, the first 3 blood days and the 7 purity days combine to make 10 days of hayd, and the last 3 blood days are istihadah.
The difference is significant: the number of hayd days changes from 6 to 10.
5
Reflect on the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence (thamarah) is the number of days counted as hayd, which affects rulings related to salah, fasting, and marital relations. This dispute directly impacts the daily practice of a Hanafi woman.
Understanding these nuances helps a mufti or faqih choose the appropriate fatwa.

مثلاً لو رأت ثلاثة أيام دماً، وعشرة طهراً ثم يوماً دماً أو بالعكس فعلى رواية ابن المبارك ومحمد الله يكون هذا الطهر فاصلاً، بمعنى أنه لا يكون حيضاً؛ لأنه يشترط فيهما كون إحاطة الدمين به في مدة الحيض، وفي هذه الصورة ليس كذلك، ففي هذه الصورة يكون ثلاثة أيام من الأوّل ومن الآخر حيضاً، وباقي الأيام استحاضة. ولو رأت يوماً دماً وخمسة طهراً ثم ثلاثة دما تكون الثلاثة حيضاً والباقي استحاضة على مذهب محمد الله؛ لأنّ الطهر المتخلّل يشترط في كونه حيضاً عنده كونه مساوياً الدمين المحيطين أو أقل، وهو مفقود هاهنا، وقس على هذا أقوال الحسن. وبالجملة؛ فكل صورة وجد فيها نصاب أحد الدمين ولم توجد الشروط المعتبرة عند أصحاب تلك الأقوال، يكون الحيض فيها هو ذلك النصاب والباقي استحاضة. (٢) قوله : وإن كان ...الخ؛ مثلاً لو رأت امرأة ثلاثة دماً، وسبعة طهراً، ثم ثلاثة دماً، يكون الثلاثة الأولى حيضاً، والباقي كله استحاضة، وذلك لفقد الشروط التي اعتبرها أصحاب تلك المذاهب، وأما عند أبي يوسف الله له ف الطهر المذكور ليس بفاصل؛ لكونه أقل من خمسة عشر يوماً، فعنده يكون العشرة حيضاً، والباقي استحاضة. So the summary is that in each of these opinions except the saying of Abu Yusuf, there is found a scenario in which the deficient purity serves as a separator, and it is that scenario in which the considered conditions are lacking, and this is not possible according to the saying of Abu Yusuf, because he does not condition non-separation by any condition. And his contemporary, the author of al-Ta'liq al-Kamil, chose, following some of his teachers, the gist of which is that 'every scenario' is a mubtada and its khabar is his saying 'be', and the fa' entering on his saying 'fafi' is for explanation. And by 'every scenario' is meant every scenario from the second division, which is when the deficient purity is more than three days, and its clarification: that the commentator divided the intervening purity into two divisions: the first: what is less than three days, so it does not separate by agreement. And the second: what is three days or more, and he mentioned in it various scenarios and diverse opinions, then he explained them, then he said: 'so in every scenario...' etc., meaning in every scenario from the second division the deficient purity is a separator in the authorization of the two companions of these opinions, except the saying of Abu Yusuf. For example: The first scenario: i.e., when the purity is fourteen days, it is a separator in their view, based on their conditions which have been mentioned, except Abu Yusuf. And in the second scenario: and it is when the purity is eight days surrounded by two blood flows within ten days, it is a separator in the narration of Ibn al-Mubarak and others. And similarly in the third scenario, which is when the purity is seven days within ten days, it is a separator according to Muhammad. And similarly in the fourth scenario: which is when the intervening purity is three days, it is a separator according to al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. And as for the last scenario: i.e., when the purity is two days surrounded by two blood flows, it falls outside the second division, so it does not separate according to anyone, just as it does not separate according to Abu Yusuf in all the mentioned scenarios; because he does not consider conditions. This, and there is another detail in the subject which is expounded in 'al-Sa'aya'. The Book of Purity / Menstruation 517: If one of the two blood flows is a nisab [minimum period], it is hayd; if each of them is a nisab, the first is hayd; if neither of them is a nisab, all is istihada. And the saying of Abu Yusuf is excepted because this does not apply according to his position. [4] [1] His saying: 'If... etc.' means: when it is established that the separation of deficient purity in all opinions except the saying of Abu Yusuf, then consider: if one of the two blood flows surrounding that purity is of the measure of nisab, i.e., three days or more up to ten days, no less, then that blood is hayd, and the rest, i.e., that purity which is not hayd legally due to the lack of some conditions, and the other blood is istihada. For example, if a woman sees three days of blood, ten days of purity, then one day of blood, or the reverse, then according to the narration of Ibn al-Mubarak and Muhammad, this purity is a separator, meaning that it is not hayd; because they condition that the two blood flows encircle it within the period of hayd, and in this scenario it is not so. So in this scenario, three days from the first and from the last are hayd, and the remaining days are istihada. And if she sees one day of blood, five days of purity, then three days of blood, then the three are hayd and the rest is istihada according to the madhhab of Muhammad; because the intervening purity is required in his view to be equal to the surrounding blood flows or less, and that is lacking here. And analogize to this the sayings of al-Hasan. In summary: every scenario in which a nisab of one of the two blood flows is found and the conditions considered by the proponents of those opinions are not found, hayd in it is that nisab and the rest is istihada. (2) His saying: 'And if... etc.' for example: if a woman sees three days of blood, seven days of purity, then three days of blood, the first three are hayd and all the rest is istihada, due to the lack of conditions which the proponents of those madhhabs considered. But according to Abu Yusuf, the mentioned purity is not a separator because it is less than fifteen days, so according to him the ten days are hayd and the rest is istihada. [3] His saying: 'All is istihada' as when she sees one day of blood, thirteen days of purity, then one day of blood; according to the five opinions, all these days are istihada. This and additional detail in these investigations is in 'al-Ta'liq al-Fasil'. [4] His saying: 'does not apply' means it does not occur according to the madhhab of Abu Yusuf; because he says that the deficient purity is absolutely not a separator.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the chapter of hayd (menstruation) from Sharh al-Wiqayah. It addresses a key issue: whether a period of 'deficient purity' (الطهر الناقص) – that is, a purity interval between two blood flows that is not a full tuhr (which would be 15 days or more) – can act as a separator (فاصل) between the two blood flows, thereby making the two blood flows independent possible hayd periods. The passage reviews the positions of several Hanafi authorities: Abu Yusuf holds that deficient purity never separates; he does not condition non-separation on any condition – any purity less than 15 days is not a separator, so the two blood flows combine into one hayd period if within 10 days. Other Hanafi scholars (Imam Muhammad, Ibn al-Mubarak, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad) accept separation in certain cases based on specific conditions: the purity must be surrounded by two blood flows within the 10-day maximum for hayd (like a blood-purity-blood pattern), and the purity must meet certain length requirements relative to the blood days. The commentator explains these conditions and gives examples. The passage then states the practical ruling: after determining if the purity separates, one looks at each blood flow: if one blood flow is a nisab (3 days or more, up to 10), it is hayd; if both are nisab, the first is hayd and the second is istihada; if neither is nisab, all is istihada. This ruling, except for Abu Yusuf's view, is based on the conditions of the other scholars. The commentary provides detailed examples illustrating the application of these different views.

Key Terms
الطهر الناقص (al-tuhr al-naqis): A purity interval between two blood flows that is less than the full purity period of 15 days (which would separate two hayd periods definitively).
نصاب (nisab): The minimum duration of hayd, which is three days in the Hanafi school.
فاصل (fasil): A separator between two blood flows, such that the blood after it is considered a new hayd period independent of the previous blood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Abu Yusuf regarding a deficient purity interval (e.g., 7 days) between two blood flows?
A) It always separates, making each blood flow independent.
B) It never separates, so the two blood flows combine into one hayd period if within the maximum hayd duration.
C) It separates only if the blood flows are each at least 3 days.
D) It separates only if the purity is surrounded by blood within 10 days.

Key Takeaway
A deficient purity interval (less than 15 days) can act as a separator in the Hanafi school only under specific conditions; otherwise, it is treated as part of the hayd period, with a significant intra-Hanafi split between Abu Yusuf and the majority.

Two Bloods Separated by Purity: The Hanafi Position and the Dispute with Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on separating purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi ruling for identifying hayd when two blood flows are separated by a purity interval, and distinguish between the majority position (Abu Hanifa/Muhammad) and the position of Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
When two bloods are separated by purity, only blood meeting the nisab (3–10 days) counts as hayd; a short tuhr does not always sever continuity.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah deals with a nuanced scenario in Hanafi taharah: a woman experiences two distinct episodes of bleeding separated by a period of purity. The matn gives a clear ruling, but the commentator expands on it, revealing a key disagreement within the Hanafi school—between the majority (following Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) and Abu Yusuf. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for correctly applying the rules of hayd and istihadah in complex cases.

Guided Path
1
State the matn's general rule
Read the matn: if one blood is nisab, it is hayd; if both are nisab, the first is hayd; if neither, all is istihadah. Understand that this rule assumes the purity interval is a valid separator.
The matn does not specify conditions for the purity—the commentator fills that gap.
2
Identify the conditions for a purity to be a separator
The commentator explains that for Muhammad (and Ibn al-Mubarak), the purity interval must be surrounded by bloods that themselves lie within the hayd period (i.e., the total span from first blood to last blood does not exceed 10 days). Additionally, the purity must equal or be less than the surrounding bloods.
These conditions apply only to incomplete purities (less than 15 days).
3
Contrast Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf holds that any purity interval less than 15 days is absolutely not a separator. Therefore, if two bloods occur within a total span of 10 days, the entire period (including the purity) becomes hayd.
This is a stricter view: it treats the whole span as one continuous hayd episode.
4
Work through the example: 3 days blood, 7 days purity, 3 days blood
According to the majority: first 3 days are hayd, the 7 days are not (they are tuhr), and the last 3 days are istihadah because they are not surrounded within a 10-day window? Actually check: according to the commentator, for this case the first 3 are hayd and the rest istihadah. According to Abu Yusuf: all 10 days are hayd.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is significant for rulings like prayer and fasting.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The matn explicitly excepts Abu Yusuf's view, and the commentator follows the majority. The mufta bihi is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: an incomplete tuhr can be a separator provided the bloods surround it within the hayd period and the purity is not longer than the bloods.
This is the relied-upon view for fatwa.

Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about how to identify hayd when two blood flows are separated by a period of purity (tuhr). The base text (matn) gives a straightforward ruling: if one blood meets the nisab (3-10 days), that blood is hayd; if both bloods are nisab, the first is hayd; if neither is nisab, all is istihadah. The commentator then explains the views of different Hanafi authorities. According to Ibn al-Mubarak and Muhammad al-Shaybani, an incomplete tuhr (less than 15 days) can still serve as a separator if the bloods surround it within the hayd period. For Abu Yusuf, however, any tuhr less than 15 days is not a separator, so the entire span from first to last blood becomes a single hayd if it does not exceed 10 days. The commentator gives examples: a woman seeing 3 days blood, 10 days purity, then 1 day blood — according to Muhammad, both bloods are hayd (3 + 1 = 4 days) and the purity is not hayd. For 3 days blood, 7 days purity, then 3 days blood — according to the dominant view, the first 3 are hayd and the rest istihadah; according to Abu Yusuf, all 10 days are hayd. The passage concludes that Abu Yusuf's view is excluded from the matn's ruling because his principle (any incomplete tuhr is non-separating) does not allow for the described scenario of a separating tuhr.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisab): The minimum duration for a blood to be considered hayd: three days to ten days (inclusive), not less.
طهر متخلل (tuhr mutakhallil): A period of purity (no bleeding) that occurs between two blood flows.
استحاضة (istihadah): Non-menstrual uterine bleeding that is not subject to the rules of hayd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The first 3 days are istihadah, the 9 days of purity are hayd, and the last 3 days are istihadah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes hayd from istihadah based on the nisab of blood and the conditions of the separating purity; Abu Yusuf's view that any short purity is not a separator is not the mufta bihi.

Primary Colors of Menstrual Blood: Red and Black
Defining primary colors of menstrual blood with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that red and black are menstrual blood by consensus and cite the hadith evidence for black.

Core Idea
Red and black are menstrual blood by consensus; black is oxidized red blood.

This lesson establishes the foundational ruling on the colors of menstrual blood. You will learn that red and black are universally accepted as hayd among Hanafis and other schools, supported by explicit Prophetic hadith and rational explanation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary colors
Recognize that redness and blackness are the two original colors mentioned explicitly as hayd.
Red is the natural color of blood; black results from intense oxidation.
2
Understand the consensus claim
The passage states 'by consensus' (ijma'an) among Hanafi imams and others, meaning this ruling is firmly established.
Ijma' here refers to agreement of the Companions and major scholars.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
Two hadiths are cited: one from Fatima bint Abi Hubaish describing menstrual blood as 'black blood, known,' and another from Abu Umama describing it as 'black, thick, with redness over it.'
These hadith are ahad (single-transmitter) but reliable for establishing the ruling.
4
Analyze the legal implication
Since black is explicitly mentioned, any dark discharge similar to black (e.g., dark brown) is also considered hayd by analogy.
This is the standard Hanafi position.

Know that the colors of menstrual blood are redness and blackness; they are menstrual blood by consensus. Similarly, strong yellowness. Reported by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i and others from the hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaish. Also al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Tabarani reported from Abu Umama marfu'an: 'Menstrual blood is black, thick, with redness over it; and istihadah blood is black and thin.' [3] His statement: 'By consensus' — i.e., among our imams and others. [4] His statement: 'Similarly, yellowness' — with dammah in Persian: zardi, al-mushba'ah (with dammah on the mim, sukun on the shin, fath on the ba), meaning strong yellowness. That is, it is also menstrual blood because in Sunan al-Bayhaqi from A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) that she used to forbid women from looking at themselves at night during menstruation and would say: 'Indeed, yellowness and cloudiness may occur.' As for what was reported by al-Bukhari and others from Umm Atiyya (may Allah be pleased with her): 'We did not consider cloudiness and yellowness as anything in the time of the Messenger of Allah' — this is interpreted as occurring after purity for a woman with a regular habit, as evidenced by the addition of Abu Dawud and al-Hakim: 'after purity, as anything.' Commentary
This passage begins a new topic on the colors of menstrual blood. The commentator first states that menstrual blood has two primary colors: red and black, which are unanimously considered hayd by Hanafis and other schools. Red is the natural color of blood; black occurs when blood is deeply oxidized. Two hadiths are cited: one from Fatima bint Abi Hubaish (reported by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i) where the Prophet describes menstrual blood as 'black blood, known,' and another from Abu Umama (reported by al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Tabarani) describing menstrual blood as 'black, thick, with redness over it' while istihadah blood is 'black and thin.' The commentator then addresses strong yellow (sufrah mushabba'ah), which is also considered hayd based on the practice of A'isha (reported by al-Bayhaqi) where she forbade women from checking for cleanliness at night during menstruation because yellowness and cloudiness (kudrah) may occur. The apparent conflict with the hadith of Umm Atiyya, where they did not count kudrah and sufrah as anything, is resolved by interpreting it as occurring after purity for a woman with a regular menstrual habit. The addition in Abu Dawud and al-Hakim clarifies that this refers to discharge seen 'after purity.' Thus, strong yellow during the days of expected menstruation is hayd, while weak yellow or cloudy discharge after complete purity (signaled by the white discharge) is not.

Key Terms
الحمرة (al-humrah): Red color; original color of blood, considered menstrual blood by consensus.
السواد (al-sawad): Black color; occurs when red blood is intensely heated/oxidized; also considered menstrual blood.
إجماعاً (ijma'an): By consensus; here meaning agreement among Hanafi imams and other scholars that red and black are hayd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Red and black are the primary colors of hayd, established by consensus and Prophetic hadith.

Strong Yellow as Menstrual Blood and Resolving the Hadith Conflict
Expanding the colors of hayd to include strong yellow and resolving evidence conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why strong yellow is considered hayd and how to reconcile the conflicting hadith of Umm Atiyya.

Core Idea
Strong yellow is hayd; weak yellow after purity is not, reconciling the hadith of A'isha and Umm Atiyya.

This lesson expands on the third color: strong yellow (sufrah mushabba'ah). You will learn that it is considered menstrual blood based on the practice of A'isha, and how the apparent contradiction with Umm Atiyya's hadith is resolved by the distinction of timing relative to purity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third color
Recognize that strong yellowness (sufrah mushabba'ah) is added to red and black as a color of hayd.
'Mushabba'ah' means saturated or intense yellow, not pale yellow.
2
Examine the evidence from A'isha
The practice of A'isha (in Sunan al-Bayhaqi) shows that she forbade women from checking for purity at night during menses because yellowness and cloudiness may appear.
This indicates that such discharges can occur during the days of hayd and are therefore part of it.
3
Analyze the conflicting hadith
Umm Atiyya reported that they did not consider yellowness and cloudiness as anything. This seems to contradict A'isha's practice.
The conflict must be resolved to determine the correct ruling.
4
Apply the reconciliation
The addition in Abu Dawud and al-Hakim clarifies that Umm Atiyya's statement refers to discharge seen 'after purity' for a woman with a regular habit.
Thus, after complete purity (marked by white discharge), any yellow or cloudy discharge is not hayd.
5
Derive the final ruling
Strong yellow during the expected days of menstruation is hayd; weak yellow or cloudy discharge after complete purity is not.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Know that the colors of menstrual blood are redness and blackness; they are menstrual blood by consensus. Similarly, strong yellowness. [1] His statement: 'Know' — when he finished detailing the author's statement: 'The intervening purity in its duration,' he began detailing his statement: 'And whatever color she sees other than whiteness.' [2] His statement: 'They are redness and blackness' — as for redness, it is evident that it is the original color of blood, and blackness occurs by intense burning, for when redness intensifies it becomes blackness. Hence it is transmitted from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The blood of menstruation is black blood, known.' Reported by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i and others from the hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaish. Also al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Tabarani reported from Abu Umama marfu'an: 'Menstrual blood is black, thick, with redness over it; and istihadah blood is black and thin.' [3] His statement: 'By consensus' — i.e., among our imams and others. [4] His statement: 'Similarly, yellowness' — with dammah in Persian: zardi, al-mushba'ah (with dammah on the mim, sukun on the shin, fath on the ba), meaning strong yellowness. That is, it is also menstrual blood because in Sunan al-Bayhaqi from A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) that she used to forbid women from looking at themselves at night during menstruation and would say: 'Indeed, yellowness and cloudiness may occur.' As for what was reported by al-Bukhari and others from Umm Atiyya (may Allah be pleased with her): 'We did not consider cloudiness and yellowness as anything in the time of the Messenger of Allah' — this is interpreted as occurring after purity for a woman with a regular habit, as evidenced by the addition of Abu Dawud and al-Hakim: 'after purity, as anything.' Commentary
This passage begins a new topic on the colors of menstrual blood. The commentator first states that menstrual blood has two primary colors: red and black, which are unanimously considered hayd by Hanafis and other schools. Red is the natural color of blood; black occurs when blood is deeply oxidized. Two hadiths are cited: one from Fatima bint Abi Hubaish (reported by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i) where the Prophet describes menstrual blood as 'black blood, known,' and another from Abu Umama (reported by al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Tabarani) describing menstrual blood as 'black, thick, with redness over it' while istihadah blood is 'black and thin.' The commentator then addresses strong yellow (sufrah mushabba'ah), which is also considered hayd based on the practice of A'isha (reported by al-Bayhaqi) where she forbade women from checking for cleanliness at night during menstruation because yellowness and cloudiness (kudrah) may occur. The apparent conflict with the hadith of Umm Atiyya, where they did not count kudrah and sufrah as anything, is resolved by interpreting it as occurring after purity for a woman with a regular menstrual habit. The addition in Abu Dawud and al-Hakim clarifies that this refers to discharge seen 'after purity.' Thus, strong yellow during the days of expected menstruation is hayd, while weak yellow or cloudy discharge after complete purity (signaled by the white discharge) is not.

Key Terms
الصفرة المشبعة (al-sufrah al-mushba'ah): Strong or saturated yellow color; legally considered menstrual blood.
الكدرة (al-kudrah): Cloudy or murky discharge; if seen after complete purity, it is not considered hayd.

The Colors of Menstrual Blood in Hanafi Fiqh: Classification and Proof
Ruling with proof on colors of menstrual blood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the colors considered menstrual blood in the Hanafi school, cite the key hadith evidence, and reconcile apparent contradictions between hadiths.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, any discharge that is not pure white is considered menstrual blood, supported by prophetic hadith.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the colors of menstrual blood (hayd). The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah establishes that red and black are universally accepted, while deep yellow, green, pale yellow, muddy, and dust-colored are also considered hayd in our school. We will analyze the hadith evidence and see how the Hanafi scholars reconcile an apparent contradiction between the hadiths of Aisha and Umm Atiyyah.

Guided Path
1
State the color categories
List all colors mentioned: red and black (consensus), deep yellow (on the more correct view), and four additional colors—green, pale yellow, muddy, and dust-colored (Hanafi position). Note the distinction between muddy (tends to white) and dust-colored (tends to black).
The passage begins with the two universally accepted colors, then expands.
2
Identify the primary hadith evidence
Extract the key narrations: (1) The Prophet's description of menstrual blood as black (Fatimah hadith). (2) Aisha's statement that yellow and muddy can occur during menstruation. (3) Umm Atiyyah's statement that they did not count yellow and muddy as anything.
The commentator uses Aisha's hadith to support yellow and muddy as hayd.
3
Analyze the reconciliation
Understand how the Hanafi response reconciles Umm Atiyyah's hadith: the addition 'after purity' (or 'after washing') in Abu Dawud and al-Hakim shows that the hadith applies only to discharge seen after the menstrual period ends, not during it.
This is the key to resolving the apparent contradiction.
4
Note the internal ikhtilaf
Recognize that even within the Hanafi school, there are differences regarding yellow. Abu Mansur al-Maturidi considers the woman's habit: if she sees yellow during purity days, it is ruled as purity. Abu Bakr al-Iskaf looks at the origin: yellow from brazilwood color is hayd, otherwise not. The more correct view (al-asahh) is that deep yellow is hayd.
The commentator refutes those who exclude yellow entirely.
5
Summarize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: during the expected menstrual period, any non-white discharge is considered hayd in the Hanafi school, affecting rulings on prayer, fasting, and marital relations. After the period ends, yellow and muddy are not hayd.
This distinction is crucial for women's practice.

[قوله: واعلم؛ لما فرغ عن تفصيل قول المصنف: والطهر المتخلل في مدته، شرع في تفصيل قوله: وما رأت من لون فيها سوى البياض. قوله: هي الحمرة والسواد؛ أما الحمرة فظاهر أنه لون أصلي للدم، والسواد يحصل بشدة الاحتراق، فإن الحمرة إذا اشتدت صارت سواداً، ولذا ورد عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: «دم الحيضة دم أسود يعرف»، أخرجه أبو داود والنسائي وغيرهما من حديث فاطمة بنت أبي حبيش. وأخرج الدارقطني والبيهقي والطبراني من حديث أبي أمامة له مرفوعاً: «دم الحيض أسود خاثر تعلوه حمرة، ودم الاستحاضة أسود ورقيق». قوله: إجماعاً؛ أي بين أئمتنا وبين غيرهم من الأئمة. قوله: وكذا الصفرة؛ بالضم بالفارسية: زردي، المشبعة بضم الميم وسكون الشين المعجمة، وفتح الباء الموحدة؛ أي الصفرة القوية، يعني: هو الحيض أيضاً لما في سنن البيهقي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنها كانت تنهى النساء أن ينظرن إلى أنفسهن ليلا في الحيض وتقول: إنها قد تكون الصفرة والكدرة. وأما ما أخرجه البخاري وغيره عن أم عطية رضي الله عنها: «كنا لا نعد الكدرة والصفرة شيئاً في عهد رسول الله»، فهو محمول على رؤيته بعد الطهر للمعتادة، بدليل زيادة أبي داود والحاكم: «بعد الطهر شيئاً». وفي رواية الدارمي: «بعد الغسل». قوله: في الأصح؛ يحتمل أن يكون معناه: أن كون الصفرة المشبعة حيضاً هو الأصح، ويحتمل أن يكون معناه كونه إجماعياً لا خلاف فيه أصح، وعلى كل ففيه إشارة إلى رد قول من لم يعد الصفرة مطلقاً من الحيض. قوله: والخضرة، بالضم بالفارسية سبزى، والصفرة الضعيفة الكدرة بالضم، يقال له بالفارسية يترى، وهو لون الماء الكدر المختلط بالغبار ونحوه. والتربية: بضم وفتح الياء، وكسر الباء الموحدة، وتشديد الياء: هو لون التراب عندنا؛ أي هذه الأربعة كلها من ألوان الحيض عندنا، وظاهره أنه لا خلاف فيه لأئمتنا، وخالف فيه غيرنا، وليس كذلك. فقد ذكر في «الهداية» وشرحها «البناية» أنه ذكر: «في المبسوط» عن أبي منصور الماتريدي: لو اعتادت أن ترى أيام طهرها صفرة، وأيام حيضها حمرة، فحكم صفرتها حكم الطهر، وقيل: إنما اعتبر ذلك في صفرة عليها بياض، وعن أبي بكر الإسكاف: فإن كانت الصفرة عن لون البقم فهو حيض، وإلا فلا. والمنقول عن الشافعي له في «مختصر المزني»: «إن الصفرة والكدرة في أيام الحيض حيض».
Know that the colors of menstruation are red and black; these two are menstruation by consensus, and likewise the deep yellow on the more correct view, and green, pale yellow, muddy, and dust-colored in our school. There is a distinction between them: muddy tends toward white, and dust-colored toward black. [Commentator says: Know that; after finishing the detail of the author's statement: 'and the purity intervening within its period', he began detailing his statement: 'and whatever color she sees other than white.' He said: 'they are red and black'; as for red, it is apparent that it is an original color of blood, and black results from intense burning; for when red intensifies it becomes black. Therefore it is reported from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The blood of menstruation is black, known' — Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i and others narrated it from Fatimah bint Abi Hubaysh. And al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Tabarani narrated from Abu Umamah marfu': 'Menstrual blood is black, thick, with redness on top; and istihadah blood is black and thin.' And in al-Darimi's narration: 'after washing.' In any case, it indicates a refutation of the one who does not count yellow at all as menstruation. And 'al-turbiyyah' — with dammah and fatha on the ya, kasra on the ba, and tashdid on the ya: it is the color of dust in our school; i.e., these four are all colors of menstruation in our school, and apparently there is no disagreement among our imams, but others differ. However, it is not so; for it is mentioned in al-Hidayah and its commentary al-Binayah that it is said in al-Mabsut from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: 'If a woman habitually sees yellow during her days of purity and red during her days of menstruation, then the ruling of her yellow is the ruling of purity.' And it was said: 'That is only considered for yellow that has whiteness.' And from Abu Bakr al-Iskaf: 'If the yellow is from the color of brazilwood, then it is menstruation; otherwise no.' And what is transmitted from al-Shafi'i in Mukhtasar al-Muzani: 'Yellow and muddy during the days of menstruation are menstruation.'
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This is a ruling-with-proof passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, discussing the colors of menstrual blood (hayd). The commentator begins by stating that the known colors of menstruation are red and black, and these are unanimously considered menstrual blood among all scholars. He then adds that deep yellow (safrah mushabba'ah) is also menstruation, but there is some difference of opinion, so it is qualified as 'on the more correct view.' The commentator provides hadith evidence: the Prophet described menstrual blood as black (hadith of Fatimah bint Abi Hubaysh), and black is merely intensified red. A hadith from Aisha indicates that yellow and muddy discharge can be menstrual. The objection from Umm Atiyyah's statement is reconciled by interpreting it as referring to discharge seen after the end of the menstrual period. The commentator then lists four more colors considered menstrual in the Hanafi school: green, pale yellow, muddy (kudrah), and dust-colored (turbiyyah). He explains the distinction: kudrah tends toward white, and turbiyyah toward black. The commentator notes that there is no disagreement among Hanafi imams on these four, but others disagree. He then mentions internal Hanafi differences regarding yellow: Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's view based on habit, and Abu Bakr al-Iskaf's view based on origin. Finally, he quotes al-Shafi'i's position that yellow and muddy during the days of menstruation are considered menstrual. The passage thus establishes the Hanafi position with proof and acknowledges some internal and external disagreements.

Key Terms
الحمرة (al-humrah): Red color; the original color of blood.
السواد (al-sawad): Black color; results from intense redness.
الصفرة المشبعة (al-sufrah al-mushabba'ah): Deep yellow; strong yellow color.
الكدرة (al-kudrah): Muddy color; tending toward white, mixed with dust.
التربية (al-turbiyyah): Dust-colored; tending toward black.

In Hanafi fiqh, any discharge that is not pure white during the menstrual period is considered hayd, with red and black universally accepted and other colors (yellow, green, muddy, dust-colored) recognized by the school based on hadith evidence.

Non-Red Discharges: The Hanafi Ruling that Yellow, Green, Muddy, and Earthy Colors are Hayd
Ruling on non-red colors as hayd with definitions and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the Hanafi school considers five non-red colors (intense yellow, weak yellow, greenish, muddy, and earthy) as menstrual blood (hayd) when they occur within the menstrual period, and cite the primary evidence from the hadith of Aisha.

Core Idea
Yellow, muddy, green, and earthy discharges are considered hayd during the menstrual period — the Hanafi school relies on Aisha's practice over a weaker narration.

This lesson addresses a common question: are non-red discharges part of menstruation? The Hanafi school answers yes, under specific conditions. We will examine the definitions of the colors and the proof that establishes this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the colors and their definitions
The passage lists five colors: intense yellow (sufra mushabba'a), weak yellow (sufra da'ifa), greenish (khudra), muddy (kudra), and earthy (turabiyya). Understand the distinction: kudra tends to white, turabiyya tends to black.
These are all considered types of hayd, not separate categories.
2
State the general Hanafi ruling
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), these colors are hayd if they appear during the usual menstrual period, regardless of whether they follow red blood.
This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, supported by most later scholars.
3
Locate the primary proof
The text cites the hadith of Aisha (رضي الله عنها) that she considered yellow and muddy as menstruation. This is preferred over the hadith of Umm Atiyya who said they were not counted.
The author states that Aisha is more knowledgeable, so her practice is given priority.
4
Understand the tarjih
The text concludes that the most correct (al-asahh) view is that these colors are hayd within the menstrual period. This is the operative position for fatwa.
The majority of Hanafi scholars (jumhur) adopted this view.
5
Note the scope
The ruling applies only to discharges that occur during the normal menstrual days. If they appear on days of purity (e.g., outside the period), they are not hayd.
A woman with a habitual cycle can determine her days.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة الحيض
في الأصح والخضرة والصَّفرة الضعيفة ، والكُدْرة والتربية عندنا، وفُرِّق ما بينهما أنَّ الكُدْرةَ ما ضرب إلى البياض والتربية إلى السواد
وفي رواية الـ ـارمي : «بعد الغسل».
[١] قوله : في الأصح ؛ يحتمل أن يكون معناه : أن كون الصفرة المشبعة حيضاً هو الأصح، ويحتمل أن يكون معناه كونه إجماعياً لا خلاف فيه أصح، وعلى كل ففيه إشارة إلى رد قول من لم يعد الصفرة مطلقاً من الحيض.
(٢) قوله : والخضرةُ ، بالضم بالفارسية سبزى، والصفرة الضعيفة الكدرة : بالضم ، يقال له بالفارسية يتركى، وهو لون الماء الكدر المختلط بالغبار ونحوه. والتربية : بضم وفتح الياء، وكسر الباء الموحدة، وتشديد الياء : هو لون التراب عندنا؛ أي هذه الأربعةُ كلّها من ألوان الحيض عندنا، وظاهره أنه لا خلاف فيه لأئمتنا، وخالف فيه غيرنا، وليس كذلك. فقد ذكر في «الهداية» وشرحها «البناية» أنه ذكر: «في المبسوط» عن أبي منصور الماتريدي : لو اعتادت أن ترى أيام طهرها صفرة، وأيام حيضها حمرة ، فحكم صفرتها حكم الطهر ، وقيل : إنّما اعتبر ذلك في صفرة عليها بياض، وعن أبي بكر الإسكاف : فإن كانت الصفرة عن لون البقم فهو حيض، وإلا فلا. والمنقول عن الشافعي له في «مختصر المزني»: «إنَّ الصفرة والكدرة في أيام الحيض حيض» أي ما روي عن عائشة أولى مما روي عن أم عطية له ، أنها قالت : «كنا لا نعد الكدرة والصفرة بعد الطهر شيئا؛ ولأن عائشة أعلم بذلك من أم عطية ، وقد يحتمل أن يكون مرادها بذلك : إذا زادت على أكثر الحيض، والله أعلم».
وأما الخضرة فقال في «البدائع»: اختلف المشايخ فيه : فقال الشيخ أبو منصور : إذا رأتها في أول الحيض كان حيضاً، وإن رأتها في آخر الحيض لا يكون حيضاً ، وجمهور الأصحاب على كونها حيضاً مطلقاً. والصحيح أن المرأة إن كانت من ذواتِ الأقراء يكون حيضاً، ويحمل على فساد الغذاء، وإن كانت كبيرة لا ترى غير الخضرة تحملُ على فساد المنبت، فلا يكون حيضاً.
وأما الكدرة وفي حكمها التربية فقال أبو يوسف : لا يكون حيضاً إلا بعد الدم، فإن رأتها في أول أيام الحيض لا تكون حيضاً، وبه قال أبو ثور وابن المنذر، وفي المقام بسط بسيط موضعه «السعاية».
وبالجملة : كون هذه الألوان حيضاً مختلف فيه بين مشايخنا وغيرهم، والأصح كونها حيضاً إذا كانت في مدة الحيض، لما روي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنها جعلت الصفرة والكدرة حيضاً.
The Book of Purity: Menstruation. The most correct is that the intense yellow, the greenish, the weak yellow, the muddy, and the earthy are [menstrual blood] according to us. The difference between them is that muddy tends towards white, and earthy towards black. In the narration of al-Darimi: 'after the bath.' [1] His statement: 'in the most correct' could mean that intense yellow being menstruation is the most correct, or it could mean that it is a matter of consensus with no disagreement, which is more correct. In any case, it indicates refuting the one who does not consider yellow as menstruation at all. (2) His statement: 'and the greenish' is with damma, in Persian 'sabzi'; and the weak yellow is 'kudra' with damma, in Persian 'yurtaki', which is the color of muddy water mixed with dust and the like. 'Turabiyya' is with damma and fatha on the ya, and kasra on the ba, and tashdid on the ya: it is the color of earth according to us. That is, these four are all colors of menstruation according to us. Superficially, there is no disagreement among our imams, but others have opposed; however, it is not so. It is mentioned in 'al-Hidaya' and its commentary 'al-Binaya' that it is mentioned in 'al-Mabsut' from Abu Mansur al-Maturidi: If a woman habitually sees yellow on her pure days and red on her menstrual days, then the ruling of her yellow is that of purity. It was said that this is considered only for yellow with whiteness. From Abu Bakr al-Isfijab: If the yellow is the color of brazilwood, it is menstruation; otherwise no. What is transmitted from al-Shafi'i in 'Mukhtasar al-Muzani': 'Yellow and muddy during the days of menstruation are menstruation.' That is, what is narrated from Aisha is more preferable than what is narrated from Umm Atiyya, that she said: 'We used not to count muddy and yellow after purity as anything.' Because Aisha is more knowledgeable of that than Umm Atiyya. It could be that she meant that if it exceeds the maximum of menstruation, and Allah knows best. As for greenish, in 'al-Bada'i' the scholars differed: Sheikh Abu Mansur said if she sees it at the beginning of menstruation, it is menstruation; if she sees it at the end, it is not menstruation. The majority of the companions hold that it is absolutely menstruation. The correct view is that if the woman is one who experiences regular cycles, it is menstruation, and it is attributed to corruption of food. If she is old and sees nothing but greenish, it is attributed to corruption of the womb, so it is not menstruation. As for muddy, and in its ruling earthy, Abu Yusuf said: It is not menstruation except after blood. If she sees it at the beginning of the menstrual days, it is not menstruation. Abu Thawr and Ibn al-Mundhir agreed. There is a detailed discussion in 'al-Si'aya'. In summary, whether these colors are menstruation is disputed among our scholars and others. The most correct is that they are menstruation if they occur within the menstrual period, due to what is narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that she considered yellow and muddy as menstruation.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage comes from a commentary on al-Wiqaya, discussing the colors of menstrual blood. The text begins by stating the relied-upon position: the intense yellow, greenish, weak yellow, muddy, and earthy colors are considered menstrual blood according to the Hanafi school. The author distinguishes between kudra (muddy, tending to white) and turabiyya (earthy, tending to black). He then notes that superficially there is consensus among the Hanafi imams, but other schools differ. The text then enters into a detailed discussion of differing opinions. It cites al-Maturidi's view that habitual yellow on pure days is treated as purity. al-Isfijab distinguishes based on the yellow's origin. It also quotes al-Shafi'i, who considers yellow and muddy as hayd during the menstrual period, and prefers the hadith of Aisha over that of Umm Atiyya. For greenish (khudra), the text notes that Abu Mansur restricts it to the beginning of menstruation, but the majority consider it hayd absolutely. The correct view distinguishes based on the woman's age and regularity: if she is of menstrual age, it is hayd; if she is old and sees only greenish, it is not hayd. For muddy and earthy, Abu Yusuf requires that it appear after blood; otherwise it is not hayd. The passage concludes that the most correct position is that these colors are hayd if they occur within the menstrual period, supported by the hadith of Aisha. This is a fine example of how the Hanafi school resolves ikhtilaf by weighing evidence (tarjih) and considering multiple factors.

Key Terms
الصفرة (al-sufra): Yellowish discharge; the text distinguishes between intense yellow (sufra mushabba'a) and weak yellow (sufra da'ifa).
الكدرة (al-kudra): Muddy color tending to white; unlike turabiyya which is earthy tending to black.
الخضرة (al-khudra): Greenish discharge; discussed separately with differing opinions among Hanafis.
التربية (al-turabiyya): Earthy color; treated like kudra.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, when are yellow (sufra) and muddy (kudra) discharges considered menstrual blood?
A) Only if they appear after red blood
B) Only if they appear at the beginning of the period
C) If they appear during the normal menstrual period
D) Only in young women

Key Takeaway
Non-red colors (intense yellow, weak yellow, greenish, muddy, earthy) are hayd when they occur within the menstrual period, as per the mufta bihi Hanafi position.
