Green and Muddy Discharges: The Hanafi Dispute on Hayd
Intra-Hanafi dispute on classification of colored discharges as hayd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Abu Mansur on khudrah and kudrah, and identify the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling.

Core Idea
Green and muddy discharges are hayd if they appear during the menstrual period – the majority Hanafi view.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the author examines a dispute among Hanafi scholars: are green (khudrah) and muddy (kudrah) discharges considered menstrual blood? Some scholars require prior blood, others distinguish by timing, but the majority and the most correct (al-ashah) position is that any non-white discharge occurring within the menstrual days is hayd. This view is supported by a hadith from Aisha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the colors under discussion
The passage mentions three types of discharge: green (khudrah), muddy (kudrah), and dust-colored (turbah). The ruling for all of them is the same.
These are discharges that are not pure red blood but have a color tint.
2
State Abu Mansur's distinction
Abu Mansur differentiates based on timing: if the discharge appears at the beginning of the menstrual period, it is hayd; if at the end, it is not.
This view introduces a temporal constraint not shared by others.
3
State Abu Yusuf's requirement
Abu Yusuf holds that these discharges are not hayd unless preceded by actual blood. If seen at the start of the period without blood, they are not hayd.
Abu Thawr and Ibn al-Mundhir (non-Hanafis) share this view.
4
Identify the majority and the relied-upon view
The majority of Hanafi mashayikh (jumhur) hold that these colors are hayd absolutely (mutlaqan) if they occur during the menstrual period. This is the most correct (al-ashah) position adopted for fatwa.
The author explicitly states: 'الأصح كونها حيضاً إذا كانت في مدة الحيض'.
5
Identify the primary proof
The primary dalil is the hadith of Aisha: 'ما سوى البياض الخالص حيضاً' – everything other than pure white is menstruation. This includes green and muddy discharges.
The hadith is narrated by Malik and Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Muwatta.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخضرة فقال في «البدائع»: اختلف المشايخ فيه : فقال الشيخ أبو منصور : إذا رأتها في أول الحيض كان حيضاً، وإن رأتها في آخر الحيض لا يكون حيضاً ، وجمهور الأصحاب على كونها حيضاً مطلقاً. والصحيح أن المرأة إن كانت من ذواتِ الأقراء يكون حيضاً، ويحمل على فساد الغذاء، وإن كانت كبيرة لا ترى غير الخضرة تحملُ على فساد المنبت، فلا يكون حيضاً.
وأما الكدرة وفي حكمها التربة فقال أبو يوسف : لا يكون حيضاً إلا بعد الدم، فإن رأتها في أول أيام الحيض لا يكون حيضاً إلا بعد الدم، فإن رأتها في أول أيام الحيض لا تكون حيضاً، وبه قال أبو ثور وابن المنذر، وفي المقام بسط بسيط موضعه «السعاية».
وبالجملة : كون هذه الألوان حيضاً مختلف فيه بين مشايخنا وغيرهم، والأصح كونها حيضاً إذا كانت في مدة الحيض، لما روي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنها جعلت ما سوى البياض الخالص حيضاً ؛ أخرجه مالك، ومن طريقه محمد بن الحسن في «الموطأ».
As for the green discharge, it is stated in al-Bada'i: The mashayikh differed about it: Abu Mansur said: If she sees it at the beginning of menstruation it is menstruation, but if she sees it at the end it is not menstruation. The majority of the companions hold that it is menstruation absolutely. The correct view is: if the woman is one of those who have regular periods, it is menstruation and is attributed to corruption of food; if she is an old woman who sees nothing but green, it is attributed to corruption of the womb, so it is not menstruation. As for the muddy discharge, and in its ruling is the dust-colored discharge, Abu Yusuf said: It is not menstruation except after blood. If she sees it at the beginning of the days of menstruation, it is not menstruation except after blood. Abu Thawr and Ibn al-Mundhir also said this. In this there is a detailed exposition in al-Si'ayah. In summary: whether these colors are menstruation is differed upon among our mashayikh and others. The most correct is that they are menstruation if they occur during the period of menstruation, due to what is narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that she considered everything other than pure white as menstruation. Reported by Malik, and through him Muhammad ibn al-Hasan in al-Muwatta.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether green (khudrah) and muddy (kudrah) discharges, as well as dust-colored (turbah), are considered menstrual blood. The author opens with the green discharge, citing al-Bada'i. There is a disagreement: Abu Mansur distinguishes between seeing it at the start or end of the period, while the majority (jumhur) hold it to be menstruation absolutely. The author then presents the sahih (correct) view: if the woman has regular cycles, it is hayd and attributed to food corruption; if she is an old woman who only sees green, it is from womb corruption and not hayd. Next, the muddy discharge: Abu Yusuf requires prior blood; if seen at the start, it is not hayd until blood appears. This view is shared by Abu Thawr and Ibn al-Mundhir (non-Hanafis). The author concludes that the most correct opinion (al-ashah) is that these colors are hayd if seen during the menstrual period, citing the hadith of Aisha that anything other than pure white is hayd. This hadith is transmitted by Malik and Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Muwatta. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of weighing opinions and relying on the primary proof.

Key Terms
الخضرة (al-khudrah): Greenish discharge from the vagina, which may or may not be considered menstrual blood.
الكدرة (al-kudrah): Muddy or turbid discharge, not clear blood.
التربة (al-turbah): Dust-colored discharge, treated in the same ruling as kudrah in this passage.
الحيض (al-hayd): Menstruation, the regular monthly discharge of blood from the womb, which imposes certain legal restrictions.
المدة (al-muddah): The time period during which menstruation can occur; the passage refers to 'muddah al-hayd' (the days of menstruation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi mashayikh, when is green discharge (khudrah) considered menstruation?
A) Only if seen at the beginning of the menstrual period
B) Only if seen after blood has appeared
C) If seen during the days of menstruation, regardless of whether blood preceded
D) Never considered menstruation

Key Takeaway
Green and muddy discharges are considered menstruation (hayd) in the Hanafi school if they occur during the menstrual period, based on the hadith that anything other than pure white is hayd.

Hayd Prevents Prayer and Fasting: Only Fasting Made Up
Explanation of the ruling that hayd prevents prayer and fasting, and only fasting is made up

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that menstruation prevents prayer and fasting and explain the intra-Hanafi debate on whether prayer becomes obligatory at the entry of time.

Core Idea
Menstruation prevents prayer, not just drops it—prayer never becomes obligatory for a menstruating woman.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya explains a core ruling of hayd: it prevents prayer and fasting, and only fasting is made up. The author contrasts two wordings and an intra-Hanafi debate about whether prayer was ever obligatory at the moment the time entered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and its Wording
Note the matn: 'يمنع الصلاة والصوم، ويُقْضَى هو، لا هي'. The author prefers 'يمنع' (prevents) over 'يسقط' (drops) to indicate no prior obligation.
This linguistic choice affects the legal analysis.
2
Locate the Proof (Dalil)
The hadith 'ما سوى البياض الخالص حيضاً' from Muwatta Malik defines hayd and triggers the rulings.
The proof is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but relied upon.
3
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Qadi Abu Zaid al-Dabusi argued that prayer becomes obligatory at the entry of time then drops; the majority hold it never becomes obligatory.
The majority's view is that 'يمنع' implies original prevention.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The author sides with the majority: prayer is never obligatory for a menstruating woman. Only fasting is made up.
This is the operative position for fatwa.
5
Note the Practical Consequence
If a woman becomes pure within a prayer time, according to the majority she does not make up that prayer; al-Dabusi would require it.
The dispute only affects cases of purification within the time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمنعُ الصَّلاة، والصوم، ويُقْضَى هو، لا هي وإنما قَدَّمَ مسألةَ الطَّهِّرِ المُتَخَلَّل على ألوان الحيض ؛ لأنها متعلقة بمدة الحيض، فألحقها بها ثُمَّ ذكر الألوان، ثُمَّ بعد ذلك شَرَعَ في أحكام الحيض، فقال: (يمنع الصلاة والصوم، ويُقْضَى هو، لا هي): أي يُقضى الصومُ لا الصَّلاة بناءً على أنَّ الحيض يمنعُ وجوبَ الصَّلاة، وصحة أدائها ما سوى البياض الخالص حيضاً؛ أخرجه مالك، ومن طريقه محمد بن الحسن في «الموطأ».
[1] قوله: وإنما قدّم...الخ؛ دفع لما يَرِدُ على المصنف له من أنه لِمَ خالف في هذا المقام ترتيب صاحب الهداية، فإنّ صاحب «الهداية» قدم ما يتعلق بالألوان، ثمَّ ذكر أحكام الحيض من سقوط الصلاة والصوم، وذكر بعدهما مسألة الطهر المتخلّل، وقدم على الكل ذكر المدة الأقل والأكثر. وحاصله: أن المصنف له لَمَّا ذكر المدَّة، وكان ذكره واجب التقديم؛ لتوقف أكثر مسائل هذا الباب على معرفتها ألحق بها مسألة الطهر المتخلّل؛ لأنها متعلقة بالمدة، بخلاف مسألة الألوان فإنّها باحثة عن كيفية الحيض لا عن مدته. فإن قلت: بحث الألوان أيضاً متعلّق بالمدَّة كما يقتضيه قول المصنف: وما رأت فيها. قلت: لا ينكر مطلق التعلق، ولو اعتبر مثل هذا فأحكام الحيض أيضاً متعلقة بالمدة، بل الغرض أن البحث عن الطهر المتخلل بحث عن نفس المدة بخلاف البحث عن اللون، فناسب تقديم بحثه عليه، ثم لما كان بحث اللون بحثاً عن كيفية الحيض تقديمه على أحكام الحيض التي هي خارجة عنه مترتبة عليه.
[1] قوله: يمنع...الخ؛ هذا أولى من قول صاحب «الهداية» وغيره أنَّ «الحيض يسقط عن الحائض الصلاة...الخ؛ لأنّ ظاهره أنه تجب عليها الصلاة ثم تبطل؛ إذ السقوط يتلو الوجوب، وإليه مالَ القاضي أبو زيد الدبوسي حيث قال: «إنّ الصلاة تجب عليها نظراً إلى الوقت، ثمّ تسقط دفعاً للحرج»، وعامة المشايخ على أن لا تجب عليها أصلاً.
It prevents prayer and fasting, and it (fasting) is made up, not it (prayer). And he only presented the issue of the intervening purity before the colors of menstruation because it is related to the duration of menstruation, so he attached it to it. Then he mentioned the colors, then after that he began the rulings of menstruation, saying: 'It prevents prayer and fasting, and it (fasting) is made up, not it (prayer)': i.e., fasting is made up, not prayer, based on the fact that menstruation prevents the obligation of prayer and the validity of its performance. Whatever is other than pure white is menstruation; reported by Malik, and through him Muhammad ibn al-Hasan in al-Muwatta. [Commentary on: 'And he only presented...']: This is a rebuttal to what may be objected to the author: why did he depart from the order of the author of al-Hidayah? For the author of al-Hidayah presented what pertains to the colors, then mentioned the rulings of menstruation regarding the dropping of prayer and fasting, and mentioned after them the issue of the intervening purity, and presented before all the mention of the minimum and maximum duration. The summary: When the author mentioned the duration, and its mention must be prioritized because most issues of this chapter depend on knowing it, he attached to it the issue of the intervening purity because it is related to the duration, unlike the issue of colors which investigate the nature of menstruation, not its duration. If you say: The investigation of colors is also related to the duration, as indicated by the author's statement 'and what she sees in it (i.e., duration)'. I say: Absolute relation is not denied, but if such were considered, then the rulings of menstruation are also related to the duration. Rather, the purpose is that the investigation of the intervening purity is an investigation of the duration itself, unlike the investigation of color. So it was appropriate to present its investigation before the colors. Then, since the investigation of color is an investigation of the nature of menstruation, it was presented before the rulings of menstruation which are external to it and dependent upon it. [Commentary on: 'It prevents...']: This wording is better than the wording of the author of al-Hidayah and others that 'menstruation drops prayer from the menstruating woman...' because its apparent meaning is that prayer becomes obligatory upon her, then becomes invalid; for dropping follows obligation. To this inclined Qadi Abu Zaid al-Dabusi, where he said: 'Prayer becomes obligatory upon her in consideration of the time, then drops to avoid hardship.' The generality of the mashayikh hold that it is not obligatory upon her at all.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of the matn statement 'يمنع الصلاة والصوم، ويُقْضَى هو، لا هي' – menstruation prevents prayer and fasting, and only fasting is made up. The author first explains why the text presented the issue of the intervening purity (tahr mutakhalil) before discussing the colors of menstruation, arguing that the intervening purity is directly about the duration of hayd, whereas colors are about its nature. He then addresses the wording of the ruling. The phrase 'يمنع' (prevents) is preferred over 'يسقط' (drops) used by al-Hidayah, because 'يسقط' implies that prayer was obligatory and then dropped. Qadi Abu Zaid al-Dabusi supported that interpretation, holding that prayer does become obligatory for a moment then drops, but the majority of Hanafi mashayikh (عامة المشايخ) hold that prayer never becomes obligatory for a menstruating woman at all. The practical consequence: according to the majority, there is no obligation to intend prayer at the start of the time; the woman is completely exempt. The passage also reaffirms the hadith that anything other than pure white is hayd, which is the basis for the ruling. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute over the juridical basis of the ruling, not over the ruling itself.

Key Terms
يمنع (yamna'): Prevents; indicates that the act (prayer) is not valid or obligatory from the start due to the state of hayd.
يقضى (yuqda): Is made up; refers to the obligation to perform the missed fasting later after purification.
الطهر المتخلل (al-tuhru al-mutakhalil): The intervening purity; a period of purity between two menstrual periods within the same menstrual days (e.g., a day of no blood between days of bleeding).
المدة (al-muddah): Duration; the minimum and maximum length of hayd (3-10 days).
الوقت (al-waqt): Time; the specific prayer time (e.g., zuhr) during which the prayer becomes due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi mashayikh, what is the ruling on prayer for a menstruating woman at the entry of the prayer time?
A) Prayer becomes obligatory upon her, then drops due to hardship
B) Prayer never becomes obligatory upon her at all
C) She must pray after she becomes pure, without making up
D) She must make up the prayer later

Key Takeaway
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars, menstruation prevents prayer entirely—it never becomes obligatory—while fasting must be made up.

Menstruation and Missed Worship: The Hanafi Ruling on Qada' of Prayer and Fasting
Explanation of the ruling on menstruation preventing prayer and fasting with only fasting requiring qada'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that menstruation prevents prayer and fasting, explain the hadith proof, and differentiate why fasting requires qada' while prayer does not.

Core Idea
Menstruation prevents the obligation of prayer entirely; only fasting must be made up because its obligation persists despite the prohibition on performance.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, the commentator clarifies a fundamental distinction in Hanafi fiqh: menstruation (hayd) prevents both prayer and fasting, but the legal consequence differs. Prayer is completely lifted—no obligation to perform or make up—while fasting, though forbidden during menstruation, remains obligatory and must be made up later. This ruling is based on the famous hadith of Aisha and a deeper legal rationale about hardship and the nature of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its components
The passage states: 'يمنع الصلاة والصوم، ويقضى هو، لا هي' (It prevents prayer and fasting, and it [fasting] is made up, not it [prayer]). Recognize that the ruling has two parts: prevention applies to both, but qada' is required only for fasting.
The term 'يمنع' (prevents) is deliberately chosen to indicate that prayer never becomes obligatory.
2
Examine the hadith proof
The commentator cites the hadith: 'كنا نحيض في عهد رسول الله ﷺ فنؤمر بقضاء الصوم ولا نؤمر بقضاء الصلاة'. This is the primary evidence. The command (فنؤمر) indicates obligation for fasting, and the negation (ولا نؤمر) indicates no obligation for prayer.
The hadith is sahih and agreed upon.
3
Understand the rationale (sirr) behind the distinction
The passage explains that fasting is only one month, so qada' of a few days is easy; prayer is daily, so requiring qada' would cause extreme hardship, especially for long periods. This reflects the Shariah principle of alleviating hardship.
The 'secret' is based on maslahah and removal of haraj.
4
Analyze the theoretical objection and response
A question arises: how can qada' be required if performance during hayd is forbidden? The answer: qada' depends on the existence of the original obligation (wujub) itself, not on the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada'). The wujub of fasting remains; only performance is temporarily prohibited.
This is a subtle usul point distinguishing between different aspects of obligation.
5
Compare with the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The commentator uses 'يمنع' to support the majority view that prayer never becomes obligatory, contrary to Qadi Abu Zaid al-Dabusi who held that it becomes obligatory and then falls. The relied-upon view is the majority one.
The ikhtilaf is about the nature of prevention (man' vs. suqut).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمنع الصلاة والصوم، ويقضى هو، لا هي. وإنما قدم مسألة الطهر المتخلل على ألوان الحيض؛ لأنها متعلقة بمدة الحيض، فألحقها بها ثم ذكر الألوان، ثم بعد ذلك شرع في أحكام الحيض، فقال: (يمنع الصلاة والصوم، ويقضى هو، لا هي): أي يقضى الصوم لا الصلاة بناء على أن الحيض يمنع وجوب الصلاة، وصحة أدائها. ما سوى البياض الخالص حيضاً؛ أخرجه مالك، ومن طريقه محمد بن الحسن في «الموطأ». فلذلك عدل عنه وقال: «يمنع»: أي يمنع الحيض الصلاة والصوم، فإن المنع أعم من أن يكون بسقوط بعد وجوب، أو بعدم وجوب. ولما كان قد يتوهم منه أن الصلاة والصوم سواسيان في الحكم صرح بالفرق بقوله: «ويقضى» بصيغة المجهول «هو»: أي الصوم أي يجب عليها قضاء الصوم بعد الطهارة، «لا هي»؛ أي لا تجب قضاء صلوات أيام الحيض. وذلك لما أخرجه مسلم عن عائشة رضي الله عنها: «كنا نحيض في عهد رسول الله ﷺ فنؤمر بقضاء الصوم ولا نؤمر بقضاء الصلاة»، وأخرجه البخاري وأبو داود والنسائي وابن ماجة والترمذي وغيرهم بألفاظ متقاربة. والسر فيه أن وجوب الصوم إنما هو في شهر واحد فلا حرج في قضاء صيام أيام معدودة منه، بخلاف الصلاة فإنها تتكرر كل يوم، فكان في أمر قضائها حرج عظيم لا سيما من كان حيضها عشرة أيام، فلذلك عفا الشارع عنها. ودل ذلك على أن الحيض يمنع وجوب الصلاة ديناً في أدائها؛ إذ لو لم يمنع الوجوب لوجب القضاء؛ فإنه لا براءة من الواجب إلا بالأداء أو بالإبراء، والصوم يجب مع الحيض، ولكن لا يمكن أداؤه لوجود المنافي، فوجب قضاؤه. فإن قلت: وجوب القضاء يستدعي وجوب الأداء؛ لأنه خلفه، والخلف إنما يجب بما يجب به الأصل، وأداء الصوم لا يجب على الحائض بل يحرم، فكيف وجوب القضاء. قلت: وجوب القضاء يستدعي سابقية نفس الوجوب لا سابقية وجوب الأداء على ما عرف في كتب الأصول.
[Menstruation] prevents prayer and fasting, and it (fasting) is made up, not it (prayer). He only presented the issue of the intervening purity before the types of menstruation because it is related to the duration of menstruation, so he attached it to it, then mentioned the types, then after that he began discussing the rulings of menstruation and said: (It prevents prayer and fasting, and it is made up, not it): meaning fasting is made up, not prayer, based on the fact that menstruation prevents the obligation of prayer and the validity of its performance. Anything other than pure whiteness is menstruation; reported by Malik, and through his chain Muhammad ibn al-Hasan in al-Muwatta'. Therefore he deviated from it and said: 'It prevents': meaning menstruation prevents prayer and fasting, for prevention is more general than being a fall after obligation or a lack of obligation. And since it could be misunderstood that prayer and fasting are the same in ruling, he clarified the difference by saying: 'And it is made up' in the passive voice 'it': meaning fasting—i.e., she must make up the fasting after purity, 'not it': i.e., it is not obligatory to make up the prayers of the days of menstruation. This is because of what Muslim reported from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): 'We used to menstruate in the time of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and we were commanded to make up the fast but not commanded to make up the prayer.' It was also reported by al-Bukhari, Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, al-Tirmidhi and others with close wording. The secret in this is that the obligation of fasting is only in one month, so there is no hardship in making up a few days of it, unlike prayer which is repeated every day, so commanding its make-up would be a great hardship, especially for one whose menstruation lasts ten days; therefore the Lawgiver waived it. And that indicates that menstruation prevents the obligation of prayer religiously in its performance; for if it did not prevent the obligation, make-up would be necessary, because there is no discharge from an obligation except by performance or by absolution. And fasting is obligatory during menstruation but cannot be performed due to the existence of the contradictory factor, so its make-up becomes obligatory. If you say: The obligation of make-up requires the obligation of performance because it is its replacement, and a replacement is only obligatory by that which makes the original obligatory, and performance of fasting is not obligatory upon a menstruating woman but rather forbidden, so how can make-up be obligatory? I say: The obligation of make-up requires the prior existence of the obligation itself, not the prior obligation of performance, as is known in the books of principles.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary explaining the ruling that menstruation prevents prayer and fasting, but only fasting requires qada (make-up). The author first clarifies why the matn author presented the issue of intervening purity (tahr mutakhallal) before the types of menstrual blood: because it relates to duration. Then he explains the matn's statement 'يمنع الصلاة والصوم' (it prevents prayer and fasting), noting that the term 'prevention' is broader than 'fall after obligation' (squq ba'd wujub) or 'non-obligation' (adam al-wujub). He deliberately used 'يمنع' to avoid the implication that prayer becomes obligatory and then falls. The author then provides the hadith from Aisha as proof: women were commanded to make up fasts but not prayers. He gives the 'secret' (al-sirr) behind the distinction: fasting is only one month, so making up a few days is easy; prayer is daily, so requiring qada would cause extreme hardship, especially for those with long menstruations. The passage then addresses a potential objection: how can qada be required for fasting if performance during menstruation is forbidden? The answer is that qada depends on the original obligation (wujub) existing at the time, not on the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada') during the period. This is a standard Hanafi usul point.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-hayd): The monthly menstrual discharge that triggers legal rulings; in this passage it is the cause for preventing prayer and fasting and requiring qada of fasting only.
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up a missed religious obligation after its prescribed time; here it is required for fasting but not for prayer.
المنع (al-man'): Prevention; the legal effect of hayd that prevents the obligation or validity of prayer and fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on qada of prayers missed due to menstruation?
A) She must make up all prayers immediately after purity.
B) She does not need to make up any prayers.
C) She must make up only the fard prayers, not the sunnah.
D) She must give expiation (kaffara) instead of making up prayers.

Key Takeaway
Menstruation prevents the obligation of prayer entirely, so no qada' is required; for fasting, the obligation persists and requires qada' after purity.

Distinction Between Hayd's Effect on Prayer and Fasting
Explaining why hayd prevents salat obligation but not sawm obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why hayd removes the obligation of prayer but not fasting, and apply the hadith to determine the ruling on qada.

Core Idea
Menstruation lifts the obligation of salat entirely, but fasting remains wajib—only performance is blocked, requiring qada.

In this passage from a Hanafi commentary on al-Wiqaya, we explore a key distinction in Islamic jurisprudence: why a menstruating woman must make up missed fasts but not missed prayers. The answer lies in both a clear prophetic hadith and a deeper wisdom about preventing hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage states that hayd prevents prayer entirely (no obligation) but only prevents performance of fasting, not its obligation. So qada of fasting is required, prayer is not.
Notice the phrase 'يمنعُ وجوب الصلاة' vs 'لا يمنع وجوب الصوم'.
2
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith from Aisha (ra) is the direct textual evidence: women were commanded to make up fasts but not prayers. This is the primary dalil.
The hadith is sahih and narrated by multiple compilers.
3
Understand the wisdom (hikmah)
Fasting is only one month, so qada is light; prayers occur daily, so qada would be burdensome, especially for long cycles. The Lawgiver alleviated hardship.
This wisdom is not the cause but explains the distinction.
4
Address the objection
The passage answers: if qada requires prior obligation, but the woman is forbidden to fast during hayd, how can she be obligated? The answer: the original obligation existed in abstract (in se), not necessarily performable at that time.
This is a subtle usul principle: obligation can exist without immediate performability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن لا يمنع وجوب الصوم، فنفس وجوبه ثابتة ، بل يمنعُ صحةً أدائه ، فيجب القضاء إذا طهرت. فلذلك عدل عنه وقال : «يمنع»: أي يمنعُ الحيض الصلاة والصوم، فإنّ المنع أعم من أن يكون بسقوط بعد وجوب، أو بعد وجوب، أو بعدم وجوب، ولما كان قد يتوهم منه أن الصلاة والصوم سواسيان في الحكم صرَّح بالفرق بقوله : «ويقضى» بصيغة المجهول «هو»: أي الصوم أي يجب عليها قضاء الصوم بعد الطهارة ، «لا هي» ؛ أي لا تجب قضاء صلوات أيام الحيض. وذلك لما أخرجه مسلم عن عائشة رضي الله عنها : «كنا نحيضُ في عهد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فنؤمر بقضاء الصوم ولا نؤمر بقضاء الصلاة» وأخرجه البخاري وأبو داود والنسائي وابن ماجة والترمذي وغيرهم بألفاظ متقاربة. والسر فيه أن وجوب الصوم إنّما هو في شهر واحدٍ فلا حرج في قضاء صيام أيام معدودة منه، بخلاف الصلاة فإنّها تتكرر كل يوم، فكان في أمر قضائها حرج عظيم لا سيما من كان حيضها عشرة أيام، فلذلك عفا الشارع عنها. ودل ذلك على أن الحيض يمنعُ وجوب الصلاة ديناً في أدائها ؛ إذ لو لم يمنع الوجوب لوجب القضاء ؛ فإنّه لا براءة من الواجب إلا بالأداء، أو بالإبراء، والصومُ يجب مع الحيض، ولكن لا يمكن أداؤه لوجود المنافي ، فوجب قضاؤه. فإن قلت : وجوب القضاء يستدعي وجوب الأداء ؛ لأنه خلفه، والخلف إنما يجب بما يجب به الأصل، وأداء الصوم لا يجب على الحائض بل يحرم، فكيف وجوب القضاء. قلت : وجوب القضاء يستدعي سابقية نفس الوجوب لا سابقية وجوب الأداء على ما عرف في كتب الأصول. قوله : فيجب ; تفريع على ثبوتِ وجوب الصوم : أي فيجب عليها قضاءُ الصوم إذا طهرت من الحيض.
But it does not prevent the obligation of fasting, for its very obligation is established, rather it prevents the validity of its performance, so making up (qada) becomes obligatory when she becomes pure. Therefore he said: 'prevents': meaning hayd prevents prayer and fasting, for prevention is more general than being by dropping after obligation, or after obligation, or by lack of obligation. And since it might be understood that prayer and fasting are equal in ruling, he clarified the difference by saying: 'And she makes up' in the passive voice 'it': meaning fasting, i.e., the qada of fasting becomes obligatory upon her after purity, 'not it': i.e., qada of the prayers of the days of hayd is not obligatory. And that is because of what Muslim narrated from Aisha: 'We used to menstruate in the time of the Messenger of Allah, and we were commanded to make up the fast and were not commanded to make up the prayer.' And it was also narrated by Bukhari, Abu Dawud, Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, Tirmidhi, and others with similar wording. The secret in it is that the obligation of fasting is only in one month, so there is no hardship in making up a few days of it, unlike prayer which recurs every day, so in commanding its qada there would be great hardship, especially for one whose hayd lasts ten days, therefore the Lawgiver granted concession from it. And that indicates that hayd prevents the obligation of prayer religiously in its performance; for if it did not prevent obligation, qada would be obligatory; for there is no release from an obligation except by performance or absolution. And fasting is obligatory during hayd, but its performance is not possible due to the existence of a preventative, so its qada becomes obligatory. If you say: The obligation of qada entails the obligation of performance, because it is its substitute, and the substitute is only obligatory by that which makes the original obligatory, and performing the fast is not obligatory on the menstruating woman, rather it is forbidden, so how can qada be obligatory? I say: The obligation of qada entails prior existence of the obligation itself, not prior existence of the obligation of performance, as is known in the books of usul. [His word: 'so becomes obligatory' is a branching from the establishment of the obligation of fasting: i.e., qada of fasting becomes obligatory upon her when she becomes pure from hayd.]
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the commentary on al-Wiqaya. It clarifies the different legal effects of menstruation (hayd) on prayer and fasting. The author begins by stating that hayd does not remove the obligation of fasting; the obligation remains, but hayd prevents the validity of performing the fast. Therefore, qada (making up) the missed fasts after purity is obligatory. The passage then explains why the wording 'prevents' was used: it encompasses both dropping an obligation after it was due and preventing obligation from arising. To avoid the misconception that prayer and fasting are treated identically, the commentator highlights the hadith from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that women were commanded to make up fasts but not prayers. The wisdom behind this distinction is that fasting is confined to one month, so making up a few days is not burdensome, whereas prayers occur daily, and requiring qada for up to ten days would cause great hardship. Consequently, the Lawgiver excused women from making up prayers. The passage further argues that hayd actually prevents the obligation of prayer itself, because if it only prevented performance, qada would still be required. For fasting, however, the obligation persists, but performance is impossible; hence qada becomes necessary. Finally, the author addresses an objection: if qada of fasting is required, it assumes the original obligation of fasting existed. But a menstruating woman is forbidden from fasting, so how can the original obligation exist? The answer is that qada only requires that the original obligation existed in itself (in abstract) at some point, not that it was immediately performable. This is a nuanced usul principle.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Menstrual blood that prevents the validity of prayer and fasting, and lifts obligation of prayer but not fasting.
قضاء (qada): Making up an obligatory act missed due to an excuse.
صوم (sawm): Fasting during Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Hadith from Aisha (ra) narrated by Muslim
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) Qiyas with janabah

Key Takeaway
Menstruating women must make up missed fasts but not missed prayers, based on the hadith and the wisdom of avoiding hardship.

The End of Prayer Time Determines Obligation for a Menstruating Woman
Ruling on when menstrual purity obligates or waives prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a menstruating woman's prayer is obligatory or waived based on the end of the prayer time, and apply the conditions regarding ghusl and takbirat al-ihram.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school considers the end of the prayer time, not the beginning, to determine if a menstruating woman must pray.

In the Hanafi school, the cause of obligation (sabab) for prayer is the part of the time that is concurrent with the act, and since the obligation is expansive (mawsu'), it extends to the end of the time. Therefore, if a woman becomes pure from menstruation at the end of the prayer time, she must pray if sufficient time remains for ghusl and takbirat al-ihram. This lesson unpacks the details and the underlying usul reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
The passage states that the consideration is with the end of the time (akhir al-waqt). This is the fundamental Hanafi rule: what matters for obligation or waiver is the state at the end of the prayer time, not the beginning.
2
Distinguish Two Scenarios Based on Purity Duration
If the woman's purity (taharah) was for ten full days (the maximum hayd), it is a certain purity, and the prayer becomes obligatory even if only a moment of time remains. If the purity was for less than ten days, it is possible that menstruation may return, so additional conditions apply.
The maximum hayd in the Hanafi school is ten days; beyond that is istihadah.
3
Apply the Condition for Less than Ten Days
For purity lasting less than ten days, the prayer is obligatory only if the remaining time after the end of menstruation is sufficient to perform the obligatory ghusl and the opening takbir (takbirat al-ihram). If only ghusl is possible but not takbir, the prayer is waived.
The time for ghusl includes its prerequisites (e.g., removing clothes, covering).
4
Understand Why Ghusl Time Is Counted from the Hayd Duration
A woman is considered pure only after performing ghusl. Therefore, the time needed for ghusl is counted from the hayd period itself, not after it. If she catches only enough time for ghusl after cessation, she has not truly become pure within the time, so prayer is not obligatory.
This is a key nuance: ghusl is part of the purification process, and the obligation depends on actual purity within the time.
5
Summarize the Usul Basis
The reasoning is rooted in usul al-fiqh: the sabab (cause) of prayer obligation is the part of the time that is concurrent with the act (al-juz' al-muqarin). Since the obligation is expansive (mawsu'), the causation extends until the end of the time, making the end the decisive moment.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the nature of expansive obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المعتبر عندنا آخر الوقت ، فإذا حاضت في آخر الوقتِ سقطت ، وإن طَهُرَتْ في آخر الوقتِ وجبت ، فإذا كانت طهارتها لعشرة وجبت الصلاة، وإن كان الباقي من الوقت لمحة، فإن كانت لأقل منها، فإن كان الباقي من الوقت مقدار ما يَسَعُ الغُسل والتَّحريمةَ وَجَبَت ، وإلا فلا ، فوقتُ الغُسْلِ يُحْتَسَبُ هاهنا من مدة الحيض. [١] قوله : فإذا...الخ ؛ تفريع على اعتبار آخر الوقت، يعني إذا حاضت المرأة في آخر وقتِ الصلاة وأدركت أوّلها وأوسطها طاهرة سقطت عنها تلك الصلاة، ولم يجب قضاؤها ، وإذا طهرت في آخر الوقت بعد ما كانت حائضاً قبله وجبت عليها تلك الصلاة، فإن لم تؤدّها في وقتها يجب قضاؤها. هو وذلك لما حقق في كتب الأصول أنّ سبب الوجوب عندنا . الجزء المقارن للصلاة، ولا تزال تنتقل السبية، وتمتد إلى آخر الوقت، لكون الوجوب موسعاً، فإذا بلغ الآخر تعين ذلك الجزء للوجوب، فوجب اعتباره. [٢] قوله : وإن كان الباقي ... الخ ؛ لأن انقطاع الدم لعشرة طهارة متيقنة ؛ لعدم زيادة الحيض على هذه المدة، فإن ما زادَ عليها استحاضة، بخلاف الانقطاع لأقل منها ، فإنّه يحتمل فيه عود الحيض لبقاء المدة، فاعتبر فيه زمان يسعُ الغسل من الحيض وابتداء تحريمة الصلاة. (٣) قوله : مقدار ما يسعُ الغسل ؛ بالضم والمراد: الاغتسال مع مقدماته كالاستقاء وخلع الثياب والتستر ونحو ذلك، وهل المراد به الغُسلُ المسنون أو المفروض الظاهر هو الثاني، صرح به ابن أمير حاج في «شرح تحرير الأصول»). [٤] قوله : من مدة الحيض ؛ وذلك لأنّ طهارتها إنّما تكون بعد الغُسل لا قبله، فإذا أدركت بعد الانقطاع زماناً لا يسعُ فيه إلا الغُسلُ لا تجب عليها قضاء تلك الصلاة ؛ لأنها لم تطهر من الحيض في الوقت حتى تجب عليها، بخلاف ما إذا كان يسعُ التحريمة أيضاً ؛ لكون التحريمة من الطهر، فيجب عليها القضاء لما تقرر في الأصول أنّ من أدرك من الوقتِ مقدار ما يسعُ التحريمة فقد وجبت عليه تلك الصلاة. (۱) (التقرير والتحبير في شرح التحرير» (٢ : ١٢٠).
Then the consideration with us is the end of the time. So if she menstruates at the end of the time, [the prayer] is waived; and if she becomes pure at the end of the time, it becomes obligatory. So if her purity is for ten days, the prayer becomes obligatory even if the remaining time is a moment; if it is for less than ten, then if the remaining time is an amount that accommodates ghusl and takbirat al-ihram, it becomes obligatory, otherwise not. The time for ghusl is counted here from the duration of menstruation. [1] His statement: 'So if...etc.' is a branching on the consideration of the end of the time, meaning: if a woman menstruates at the end of the prayer time while she was pure at its beginning and middle, that prayer is waived for her, and she does not have to make it up; and if she becomes pure at the end of the time after having been menstruating before it, that prayer becomes obligatory on her; if she does not perform it in its time, she must make it up. He said: That is because it has been established in the books of usul that the cause of obligation with us is the part that is concomitant with the prayer, and the causation continues and extends until the end of the time because the obligation is expansive. When the end is reached, that part becomes specified for obligation, so it must be considered. [2] His statement: 'And if the remaining...etc.' because the cessation of blood for ten days is a certain purity, since menstruation does not exceed this period; what exceeds it is istihadah, unlike cessation for less than ten, because it is possible for menstruation to return due to the remaining period, so a time that accommodates ghusl from menstruation and the beginning of takbirat al-ihram is considered. (3) His statement: 'an amount that accommodates ghusl' – with dammah, meaning the washing with its prerequisites like drawing water, removing clothes, covering, etc. Is the intended meaning the recommended ghusl or the obligatory? The apparent is the latter, as stated by Ibn Amir Hajj in Sharh Tahrir al-Usul. [4] His statement: 'from the duration of menstruation' – because her purity only occurs after ghusl, not before. So if after cessation she catches a time that only accommodates ghusl, she does not have to make up that prayer, because she did not become pure from menstruation within the time such that it becomes obligatory. Unlike if it also accommodates takbirat al-ihram, because takbirat al-ihram is from purity, so she must make it up, as established in usul that whoever catches from the time an amount that accommodates takbirat al-ihram, that prayer has become obligatory on him.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn ruling about the consideration of the end of the prayer time for menstruation. The author explains that in the Hanafi school, the determining factor for whether a prayer is obligatory or waived for a menstruating woman is the end of the prayer time, not the beginning. If she menstruates at the end of the time, the prayer is completely waived. If she becomes pure at the end, she must pray if there is enough time for ghusl and takbirat al-ihram. For purity lasting ten full days (the maximum hayd), the obligation is immediate even for a moment; for less than ten, the time must accommodate both the ritual bath and the opening takbir. The reasoning is based on usul al-fiqh: the cause (sabab) of obligation is the part of time that coincides with the prayer, and since the obligation is expansive (mawsu'), the cause continues until the end of the time, when it becomes fixed. The commentary also clarifies that 'ghusl' here means the obligatory (fard) ghusl, not the recommended one, and that the time for ghusl is counted from the duration of hayd itself, because one is not considered pure until after performing ghusl.

Key Terms
طهارة لعشرة (taharat li-asharah): Purity from menstruation for ten consecutive days; considered definitive end of hayd because maximum hayd is ten days.
تحريمة (tahrimah): The opening takbir (takbirat al-ihram) of the prayer; one of its pillars.
غسل (ghusl): The obligatory ritual bath required after menstruation; specified here as fard ghusl, not sunnah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a woman becomes pure from menstruation (having had a period of less than ten days) and there is only enough time to perform ghusl but not the takbirat al-ihram, what is the ruling on the prayer?
A) The prayer is obligatory and must be performed immediately.
B) The prayer is waived; she does not have to make it up.
C) She must perform the prayer later as qada.
D) She must perform ghusl and then pray, even if takbir is missed.

Key Takeaway
The end of the prayer time is decisive in Hanafi fiqh for a menstruating woman's prayer obligation, with the condition of sufficient time for ghusl and takbirat al-ihram for periods under ten days.

Rulings on Menstruation: Obligatory vs Voluntary in Fasting and Prayer
Distinction between obligatory and voluntary acts when menstruation occurs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a menstruating woman must make up an interrupted fast or prayer, distinguishing between obligatory and voluntary acts, and explain the rationale for the difference.

Core Idea
Voluntary prayer interrupted by menstruation must be made up; voluntary fast does not – because only prayer becomes binding upon commencement.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: when a woman menstruates during an act of worship, the ruling depends on whether the act is obligatory or voluntary, and whether it is prayer or fasting. While both obligatory and voluntary acts are invalidated by menstruation, the requirement to make them up differs. The author unpacks the underlying principles: for obligatory fasts, the obligation remains; for obligatory prayers, the end of the time is the criterion; for voluntary prayers, commencing makes them binding; for voluntary fasts, no such binding occurs. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly advising women on their religious duties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
Determine whether the act interrupted by menstruation is fasting or prayer, and whether it is obligatory (fard/wajib) or voluntary (nafl/sunnah/mustahabb). The text gives four combinations: obligatory fast, voluntary fast, obligatory prayer, voluntary prayer.
Obligatory includes Ramadan, specified vows; voluntary includes Ashura, Arafah, white days, etc.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For obligatory fast: qada wajib. For voluntary fast: no qada. For obligatory prayer: no qada (waived). For voluntary prayer: qada wajib due to luzum bi al-shuru'.
The ruling for obligatory prayer is based on the end of the time being the criterion.
3
Locate the dalil for each ruling
The text provides the proofs: for obligatory fast, the obligation remains; for voluntary fast, no obligation arises; for obligatory prayer, the end of time matters; for voluntary prayer, it becomes binding upon commencement.
These are rational proofs (dalil aqli) drawing on established principles.
4
Apply the principle of luzum bi al-shuru'
Note that this principle applies only to voluntary prayer, not voluntary fast. If a voluntary act is binding once started, qada is required; if not, it is waived.
The Hanafi school distinguishes between acts that become binding upon commencement and those that do not.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
The difference appears in the obligation of qada and also in the obligation of performance if time is ample or if a miracle occurs. Additionally, if purity lasts ten days, the time for ghusl counts as purity.
This shows the real-world impact of the rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصَّائمة إذا حاضت في النَّهار، فإن كان في آخره بطل صومها، فيجب قضاؤه إن كان صوماً واجباً ، وإن كان نفلاً لا ، بخلافِ صلاةِ النفل " إذا حاضت في خلالها، فإنها تبطل ويجب قضاؤها. وتظهر ثمرته في وجوب قضائها، وفي وجوب أدائها أيضاً إن اتسع الوقت، أو أمكن له أداء العبادة الكثيرة في الزمان القليل بخرق عادة كما وقع لكثير من الأولياء، وهذا كله بخلاف ما إذا انقطع لعشرة ، فإنّ زمانَ الغُسل هناك محسوب من الطهر، وإلا لَزِمَ أن تزيد مدة الحيض على عشرة. [١]قوله : وإن كان نفلاً ؛ المراد به ما يعم المسنون، والمستحب، وغيرهما : كصوم عاشوراء، وعرفة، وأيام البيض، وغيرها. [٢]قوله : بخلاف صلاة النفل (۱) ؛ تفصيلُ المقام : أنّ الحائض في أثناء الصومِ والصلاة إما أن يكون صومها وصلاتها فرضاً، وإما أن يكون نفلاً، فإن كان الصوم فرضاً بطل صومه ذلك بطريان الحيض، ووجب عليه قضاؤه : أي أداء ما كان واجباً عليه ؛ لبقائه عليه، فإنّ الواجب إذا فسد لم يجب عليه إلا أداء ذلك الواجب مرة أخرى كما صرح به في «الأشباه والنظائر» وغيره. فإن كان الواجب مؤقتاً كالنذر المعين، وصوم رمضان ونحو ذلك، كان أداؤه بعد الطهارة وبعد ذهاب الوقتِ قضاء حقيقة، وإن لم يكن مؤقتاً كصوم النذر المطلق سمي ذلك قضاء مسامحة. وإن كانت الصلاة فرضاً سقطت عنه تلك الصلاة وبطلت، ولم يجب عليها قضاؤها بناءً على ما تقرر عندنا أنّ المعتبر هو آخر الوقت، فإذا طرأ الحيض في أثناء الوقت، ولو في أثناء الصلاة سقطت عنه تلك الصلاة. كذا في «فتح القدير». وإن كانت صلاتها التي حاضت في أثنائها نفلاً بطلت تلك الصلاة، ووجب عليها قضاؤها ؛ للزومها بالشروع ، وإن كان الصوم الذي حاضت في أثنائه نفلاً وجبَ (۱) قال أخي جلبي في ذخيرة العقبى (ص) (٦٠) : وقد صرح به صاحب «الخلاصة» حيث قال : ولو افتتحت الصلاة في آخر الوقت ثم حاضت لا يلزمها قضاء هذه الصلاة، بخلاف التطوع، فإنه لو أدركها الحيض بعدما افتتحت صلاة التطوع كان عليها قضاء تلك الصلاة إذا طهرت».
And the fasting woman, if she menstruates during the day: if it is at the end, her fast becomes invalid; so she must make it up if it was an obligatory fast, and if it was voluntary, no [make-up]. Unlike voluntary prayer: if she menstruates during it, it becomes invalid and she must make it up. The benefit appears in the obligation of its qada, and also in the obligation of its performance if the time is ample, or if it is possible for her to perform many acts of worship in a short time by a breaking of the norm as occurred for many saints. All this is contrary to the case where [the bleeding] stops for ten days: then the time for ghusl is counted from purity, otherwise the duration of menstruation would exceed ten days. [1] His statement: 'And if it was voluntary' means what includes the sunnah, mustahabb, and others: like the fast of Ashura, Arafah, the white days, etc. [2] His statement: 'Unlike voluntary prayer' (1); the explanation of the matter: The menstruating woman during fasting and prayer is either that her fast and prayer are obligatory or voluntary. If the fast is obligatory, her fast is invalidated by the occurrence of menstruation, and she must make it up: that is, perform what was obligatory on her because it remains incumbent on her. For when an obligation becomes corrupt, nothing is required except performing that obligation again, as stated in Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir and others. If the obligation is time-bound, like a specified vow, Ramadan fast, etc., then its performance after purity and after the time has passed is qada in reality; if not time-bound, like an absolute vow, it is called qada by way of leniency. If the prayer is obligatory, that prayer is waived and becomes invalid, and she does not have to make it up, based on what is established with us that the consideration is the end of the time. So if menstruation occurs during the time, even during the prayer, that prayer is waived. So in Fath al-Qadir. And if her prayer in which she menstruated was voluntary, that prayer becomes invalid and she must make it up because it became binding by commencement. And if the fast in which she menstruated was voluntary, it becomes... (1) My brother Jalabi said in Dhakhirat al-Uqba (p. 60): The author of Al-Khulasah stated: 'If she begins the prayer at the end of the time then menstruates, she is not obliged to make up that prayer, unlike the voluntary prayer: if menstruation overtakes her after she has begun a voluntary prayer, she must make up that prayer when she becomes pure.'
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling comparing menstruation's effect on fasting and prayer, distinguishing between obligatory and voluntary acts. The author states that if a menstruating woman is fasting and her period begins at the end of the day, the fast is invalidated. For an obligatory fast (e.g., Ramadan, a specified vow), she must make it up later. For a voluntary fast (e.g., Ashura, Arafah), no make-up is required. This differs from voluntary prayer: if menstruation occurs during a voluntary prayer, that prayer is invalidated and must be made up. The commentary elaborates: for obligatory fasts, qada is wajib because the obligation remains; for obligatory prayers, qada is not required even if menstruation occurs during the prayer, because the end of the time is the criterion—if she is menstruating at the end, the prayer is waived. For voluntary prayers, they become binding once started (ilzam bil-shuru'), so they must be made up. The passage also notes that if purity is for ten days, the ghusl time is counted as part of purity, otherwise the hayd period would exceed ten days. A footnote clarifies that if a woman begins a prayer at the end of the time and then menstruates, she does not have to make up an obligatory prayer, but she must make up a voluntary prayer.

Key Terms
صوم واجب (sawm wajib): An obligatory fast, such as Ramadan or a specified vow; if invalidated by menstruation, qada is required.
صوم نفل (sawm nafl): A voluntary fast, including sunnah and mustahabb fasts; if invalidated by menstruation, no qada is required.
لزوم بالشروع (luzum bi al-shuru'): The principle that once a voluntary act of worship (like nafl prayer) is commenced, it becomes binding (lazim) to complete it, and if invalidated, must be made up.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman begins a voluntary (nafl) prayer, but before finishing she menstruates. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) The prayer is invalid and she does not need to make it up.
B) The prayer is invalid and she must make it up after purification.
C) The prayer remains valid if she completes it quickly.
D) The prayer is waived entirely like an obligatory prayer.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, women must make up voluntary prayers interrupted by menstruation, but not voluntary fasts, because only prayer becomes binding upon commencement.

Menstruation and Nafl Fasting: The Qada Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qada for nafl acts interrupted by menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the initial Hanafi ruling on qada for nafl fast vs. nafl prayer interrupted by menstruation, and explain the reasoning from Fath al-Qadir and Dhakhirat al-Uqba.

Core Idea
A nafl fast interrupted by menstruation does not require qada in the base text; but Fath al-Qadir disputes this, arguing both nafl acts bind equally.

This passage examines a subtle intra-Hanafi disagreement: does a woman who menstruates during a supererogatory fast need to make it up? The matn distinguishes between fast and prayer, but later scholars challenge this. We will trace the reasoning and see why the commentator remains troubled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
Read the matn's statement: 'If she was fasting supererogatorily (nafl), no [qada]. This is contrary to supererogatory prayer.' What is the initial distinction?
The matn makes a clear difference between nafl fast and nafl prayer.
2
Understand the basis for the distinction
The commentary explains that for obligatory acts, fasting requires qada but prayer does not (due to end-of-time consideration). For nafl, the matn's distinction is not fully justified. Look at the reasoning: nafl prayer becomes binding by commencement, but why not nafl fast?
The commentator highlights the principle of luvsum (bindingness) and its role.
3
Examine the critique from Fath al-Qadir
Fath al-Qadir rejects the distinction entirely. Both acts are invalidated by menstruation and both are binding by commencement. Why would there be a difference?
This critique is a direct challenge to the matn's position.
4
Analyze the reconciliation by Dhakhirat al-Uqba
Akhi Jilabi attempts to preserve the distinction: starting a nafl fast while menstruating is a prohibited act, so it does not create a valid binding obligation. Starting a nafl prayer while menstruating is not directly prohibited (only the act of prayer itself is prohibited, not the commencement), so it binds. Evaluate this argument.
The commentator finds this reasoning problematic, as it conflicts with the general principle of luvsum.
5
Evaluate the commentator's final stance
The commentator concludes that the difference remains problematic because the general rule is that any nafl act, once commenced, becomes binding and any nullifier requires qada. No satisfactory distinction has been made.
The dispute is left unresolved in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصَّائمة إذا حاضت في النَّهار، فإن كان في آخره بطل صومها، فيجب قضاؤه إن كان صوماً واجباً ، وإن كان نفلاً لا ، بخلافِ صلاةِ النفل " إذا حاضت في خلالها، فإنها تبطل ويجب قضاؤها وتظهر ثمرته في وجوب قضائها، وفي وجوب أدائها أيضاً إن اتسع الوقت، أو أمكن له أداء العبادة الكثيرة في الزمان القليل بخرق عادة كما وقع لكثير من الأولياء، وهذا كله بخلاف ما إذا انقطع لعشرة ، فإنّ زمانَ الغُسل هناك محسوب من الطهر، وإلا لَزِمَ أن تزيد مدة الحيض على عشرة. [١]قوله : وإن كان نفلاً ؛ المراد به ما يعم المسنون، والمستحب، وغيرهما : كصوم عاشوراء، وعرفة، وأيام البيض، وغيرها. [٢]قوله : بخلاف صلاة النفل (۱) ؛ تفصيلُ المقام : أنّ الحائض في أثناء الصومِ والصلاة إما أن يكون صومها وصلاتها فرضاً، وإما أن يكون نفلاً، فإن كان الصوم فرضاً بطل صومه ذلك بطريان الحيض، ووجب عليه قضاؤه : أي أداء ما كان واجباً عليه ؛ لبقائه عليه، فإنّ الواجب إذا فسد لم يجب عليه إلا أداء ذلك الواجب مرة أخرى كما صرح به في «الأشباه والنظائر» وغيره. فإن كان الواجب مؤقتاً كالنذر المعين، وصوم رمضان ونحو ذلك، كان أداؤه بعد الطهارة وبعد ذهاب الوقتِ قضاء حقيقة، وإن لم يكن مؤقتاً كصوم النذر المطلق سمي ذلك قضاء مسامحة. وإن كانت الصلاة فرضاً سقطت عنه تلك الصلاة وبطلت، ولم يجب عليها قضاؤها بناءً على ما تقرر عندنا أنّ المعتبر هو آخر الوقت، فإذا طرأ الحيض في أثناء الوقت، ولو في أثناء الصلاة سقطت عنه تلك الصلاة. كذا في «فتح القدير». وإن كانت صلاتها التي حاضت في أثنائها نفلاً بطلت تلك الصلاة، ووجب عليها قضاؤها ؛ للزومها بالشروع ، وإن كان الصوم الذي حاضت في أثنائه نفلاً وجبَ عليها قضاؤه أيضاً ؛ للزومه بالشروع، صرح به في «البعـ" و«النهاية» و«شرح مختصر الطحاوي» للإسبيجابي. (1) نقلا. عن «فتح القدير» وحكم بأن ما ذكره الشارح صدر الشريعة من الفرق بين الصوم النفل والصلاة النفل غير صحيح، فإنّه لا يظهرُ الفرق بينهما لبطلانهما بالحيض، وكونهما لازمين بالشروع، وما لزم بالشروع وجب عليه قضاؤه بطريان المفسد. ووجه أخي جلبي في ذخيرة العقبي» الفرق: «بأنّ التي شرعت الصوم حائضاً لا يجب عليها صونُ ما أدت ؛ لأنها بمجرد الشروع فيه تصير مشابهة للمنهي . عنه ؛ ولذا تسمّى صائمة بالشروع في الجزء الأول، ألا ترى أنه يحنث به إذا حلفت أن لا تصوم، فلما لم يجب عليها صون المؤدَّى بمباشرة المنهي عنه لم يجب عليها القضاء ؛ لأنَّ وجوبه مبني على وجوب الأداء بخلاف الشروع في الصلاة. فإنّها وإن شرعت فيها حائضاً بناءً على أنّ المفسد في أوله وآخره سواء، لكنَّ الشروع فيها ليس بمجاور للمنهي عنه ؛ لأنَّ التحريم عقد على أدائها، وليس بأداء ؛ لأنّ أداءها لا يكون إلا بالتقييد بالسجدة ؛ ولهذا لا يحنث به من حلف أن لا يصلِّي ما لم يقيد بالسجدة، فلما كان الشروع صحيحاً هاهنا وجب عليها صون المؤدّى فيجب القضاء». انتهى (۲) ولا يخفى عليك أنّ هذا كله مع ما فيه على ما سيطلع عليه من يطالع «السعاية» لا يدفعُ الإشكال، وهو أنه قد تقرّر في الأول أن النفل مطلقاً يلزم بالشروع، وأنّ طَرَيان مفسد له مطلقاً صوماً كان أو صلاة ، وإنّ الواجب في الذمة سواء كان بالشروع المفسد أو بغيره لا تبرأ عنه الذمة إلا بالأداء أو الإبراء، فبناءً على هذا الفرق مشكل.
The fasting woman, if she menstruates during the day: if it is at its end, her fast becomes invalid, so she must make it up if it was an obligatory fast, but if it was supererogatory (nafl), then no. This is contrary to supererogatory prayer: if she menstruates during it, it becomes invalid and she must make it up. And its result appears in the obligation of making it up, and also in the obligation of performing it if the time is spacious, or if it is possible for her to perform many acts of worship in a short time by breaking habit, as occurred for many saints. All of this is contrary to the case where the bleeding stops for ten days, for the time of ghusl there is counted from purity, otherwise the duration of menstruation would necessarily exceed ten days. [1] His statement: 'And if it was supererogatory' means what includes recommended, desirable, and others, like the fast of Ashura, Arafah, the white days, and others. [2] His statement: 'Contrary to supererogatory prayer' — the detail of the situation: The menstruating woman during fasting and prayer either her fast and prayer are obligatory or supererogatory. If the fast is obligatory, her fast becomes invalid by the occurrence of menstruation, and she must make it up, i.e., perform what was obligatory upon her because it remains upon her. For when an obligatory act becomes corrupt, nothing is obligated except performing that obligatory act once again, as stated in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir and others. If the obligatory act was time-bound, like a specific vow and the fast of Ramadan and the like, then performing it after purity and after the time has passed is literally a make-up (qada). If it was not time-bound, like an absolute vow, it is called a make-up by way of leniency. If the prayer was obligatory, that prayer is dropped and becomes invalid, and she does not have to make it up based on what is established with us that the consideration is the end of the time. So if menstruation occurs during the time, even during the prayer, that prayer is dropped from her. This is stated in Fath al-Qadir. And if her prayer during which she menstruated was supererogatory, that prayer becomes invalid and she must make it up because it becomes binding by commencement. And if the fast during which she menstruated was supererogatory, she must also make it up because it becomes binding by commencement. This is stated in al-Bahr [al-Ra'iq], al-Nihayah, and Sharh Mukhtasar al-Tahawi by al-Asbijabi. (1) Quoted from Fath al-Qadir, and it ruled that what the commentator Sadr al-Shari'ah mentioned of the difference between supererogatory fast and supererogatory prayer is incorrect, for no difference appears between them because both are invalidated by menstruation, and both are binding by commencement, and what is binding by commencement must be made up when a nullifier occurs. And the reasoning of Akhi Jilabi in Dhakhirat al-Uqba for the difference: 'The one who commenced the fast while menstruating is not obligated to preserve what she performed, because by the mere commencement she becomes similar to the prohibited act; therefore she is called a fasting person by commencement in the first part. Do you not see that she would be guilty of a vow if she swore not to fast? So when she is not obligated to preserve what is performed by engaging in the prohibited act, she is not obligated to make it up, because the obligation of making it up is based on the obligation of performance, unlike commencement in prayer. For even if she commenced it while menstruating based on the fact that the nullifier in its beginning and end is the same, yet the commencement is not adjacent to the prohibited act, because the prohibition is attached to its performance, not to the commencement; because its performance is only achieved by completing it with prostration. Therefore, one who swears not to pray is not guilty of the vow unless she completes it with prostration. So when the commencement is valid here, she is obligated to preserve what is performed, so she must make it up.' End. (2) And it is not hidden from you that all of this, with what it contains, as will be seen by whoever peruses al-Si'ayah, does not remove the problem, which is that it has been established earlier that supererogatory acts absolutely become binding by commencement, and that the occurrence of a nullifier absolutely for them, whether fast or prayer, and the obligation upon the conscience whether by the nullifying commencement or otherwise, is not discharged except by performance or absolution. So based on this, the difference is problematic.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a supererogatory (nafl) fast interrupted by menstruation requires qada (make-up) or not. The base matn (likely from al-Wiqayah) states that a nafl fast does not require qada, but a nafl prayer does. The commentator explains that for obligatory acts, qada is required for fasting but not for prayer due to the consideration of the end of time. Then he cites a view from Fath al-Qadir that rejects the distinction between nafl fast and nafl prayer, arguing both are binding once commenced. Akhi Jilabi in Dhakhirat al-Uqba offers a nuanced reconciliation: starting a nafl fast while menstruating is itself a prohibited act, so it does not create a binding obligation, whereas starting a nafl prayer while menstruating is not directly prohibited as an act itself. The commentator remains unconvinced, noting that the general principle of luvsum (bindingness) applies to all supererogatory acts, making the difference problematic. The discussion shows the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning and the existence of differing positions within the school.

Key Terms
حائض (ha'id): A woman experiencing menstrual bleeding, which nullifies the validity of fasting and prayer.
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory act of worship, not obligatory but recommended.
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed or invalidated act of worship after its prescribed time.
شروع (shuru'): The act of commencing an act of worship, which in Hanafi law makes the act binding (lazim) even if it was originally nafl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the position of Fath al-Qadir?
A) It agrees with the matn.
B) It holds that both nafl fast and nafl prayer require qada.
C) It holds that neither requires qada.
D) It holds that only nafl prayer requires qada.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the bindingness of a commenced nafl act is a general principle, but its application to menstruation during nafl fast is disputed.

Invalidity of Fast for a Woman Who Becomes Pure During the Day
Matn ruling with commentary on fasting and hayd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a woman who becomes pure from hayd during the day cannot validly fast that day, and explain that purity from dawn to sunset is a condition for fasting.

Core Idea
The fast is invalid if even a moment of the day is spent in hayd.

This lesson examines a specific ruling regarding menstruation (hayd) and fasting. A woman whose menstrual bleeding stops during the day may wonder if she can salvage the fast by intending from that moment. The classical Hanafi text clarifies that the fast of that day is entirely invalid, regardless of when purity occurs. Understanding the condition of purity from the start of the day is crucial for applying the rule correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act in question is a woman observing a fast on a day when she was menstruating at dawn but becomes pure later. The ruling is that the fast is invalid (batil).
2
Identify the Condition of Purity
For a fast to be valid, the person must be in a state of ritual purity (free from hayd or nifas) for the entire day from dawn to sunset. If any part of the day is spent in hayd, the condition is not met.
This is a shart (condition) for the validity of the fast.
3
Explain Why the Fast Is Invalid
The fast is invalid because the condition of purity was absent at the beginning of the day. The day started while she was menstruating, so the entire fast is void, even if she later becomes pure and has not eaten.
The commentary explicitly states that hayd is fundamentally incompatible (munafi) with the validity of the fast.
4
Note the Practical Consequence
Since the fast is invalid, she must make up (qada) that day after Ramadan. She cannot consider it a valid fast even if she intended after becoming pure.

Source Text

١ . وإن طَهُرَتْ في النهار ، ولم تأكل شيئا لا يجزئ صومُ هذا اليوم (۳) [١]قوله : ولم تأكل شيئاً ; أي من طلوع الفجر إلى ذلك الوقت. هذا [٢] قوله : لا يجزئ صوم اليوم ; أي وإن طهرت قبل نصف النهار الشرعي، ونوت الصوم ; وذلك لأنّ الحيض وكذا النفاس منافٍ لصحة الصوم مطلقاً، فإنّ

[2] His statement: 'the fast of the day is not valid' means even if she became pure before the middle of the day in the shar'i sense, and intended to fast; and that is because menstruation and postpartum bleeding are incompatible with the validity of fasting absolutely, for...
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn, explained by the commentator. It addresses the case where a woman who was menstruating becomes pure (i.e., her bleeding stops) during the day, before the end of the day. The ruling is that she cannot make that day a valid fast, even if she becomes pure before noon (the shar'i middle of the day) and has not eaten or drunk anything since dawn. The reason is that the day began while she was in a state of hayd (menstruation) or nifas (postpartum bleeding), which fundamentally contradicts the validity of fasting. Since fasting requires the entire day to be free of these conditions, the fast is invalid from the start. If the day was a Ramadan day, she must make it up later. The cut-off sentence suggests further explanation, likely that purity must be present from the beginning of the day.

Key Terms
طَهُرَتْ (tahurat): She became pure from menstruation, meaning her bleeding stopped completely.
نصف النهار الشرعي (nisf al-nahar al-shar'i): The shar'i middle of the day, computed as half the time between dawn and sunset.

Key Takeaway
A woman who was menstruating at dawn cannot validly fast that day, regardless of when she becomes pure.

The Ruling on a Woman Who Becomes Pure During the Day While Fasting
Ruling on a woman who becomes pure during the day

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a menstruating woman who becomes pure during the day while fasting and explain why she must make up the fast.

Core Idea
A fast becomes binding upon commencement; an invalid fast due to menstruation requires qada.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a woman begins fasting while menstruating (or becomes pure during the day) and has not eaten. According to the Hanafi school, her fast is invalid, but because she commenced the fast, it becomes binding and must be made up. The passage also critiques a view that attempted to distinguish between voluntary fast and voluntary prayer regarding the obligation of qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The scenario is a woman who becomes pure from menstruation or postpartum bleeding during the day, and she has not eaten anything since dawn.
Even if she refrains from eating, her fast is not valid because purity is a condition from the start.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is clear: her fast for that day is not valid (la yujzi') and she must make it up (qada').
The text explicitly says 'لا يجزئ صوم هذا اليوم عليها قضاؤه أيضاً'.
3
Understand the legal basis
The basis is the principle of luzūm bil-shurūʿ (binding by commencement). Because she started the fast, it becomes a binding obligation to complete it. If an invalidator (like the impurity present at the start) occurs, the fast is invalid but qada is required.
This principle applies to both voluntary and obligatory acts that are commenced.
4
Recognize the internal dispute
The passage cites a criticism of Sadr al-Shari‘ah, who allegedly tried to differentiate between voluntary fast and voluntary prayer regarding qada. The author rejects this, affirming that both are invalidated by menstruation and both become binding upon commencement.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; the approved view is that no distinction exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١ . وإن طَهُرَتْ في النهار ، ولم تأكل شيئا لا يجزئ صومُ هذا اليوم عليها قضاؤه أيضاً ؛ للزومه بالشروع، صرح به في «البحر» و«النهاية» و«شرح مختصر الطحاوي» للإسبيجابي. (1) نقلا. عن «فتح القدير» وحكم بأن ما ذكره الشارح صدر الشريعة من الفرق بين الصوم النفل والصلاة النفل غير صحيح، فإنّه لا يظهرُ الفرق بينهما لبطلانهما بالحيض، وكونهما لازمين بالشروع، وما لزم بالشروع وجب عليه قضاؤه بطريان المفسد.
1. And if she becomes pure during the day, and she did not eat anything, the fast of this day is not valid, and making it up is also required of her; because it became binding by commencement. It is stated explicitly in 'Al-Bahr', 'Al-Nihayah', and 'Sharh Mukhtasar al-Tahawi' by al-Isbijabi. Transmitted from 'Fath al-Qadir'. And it ruled that what the commentator Sadr al-Shari'ah mentioned regarding the difference between a voluntary fast and a voluntary prayer is incorrect, for the difference between them does not appear because both are invalidated by menstruation, and both become binding by commencement, and what becomes binding by commencement requires making it up if an invalidator occurs.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for a woman whose menstruation ends during the day while she is fasting. If she has not eaten anything since dawn, her fast for that day is not valid because the presence of menstruation at the beginning of the day invalidates the entire fast. However, since she began the fast validly (thinking she was pure) or due to the general principle that a voluntary fast becomes binding upon commencement, she must make up that day. The author cites several authorities: Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Al-Nihayah, and Sharh Mukhtasar al-Tahawi. He then quotes from Fath al-Qadir a critique of Sadr al-Shari'ah's attempt to differentiate between voluntary fast and voluntary prayer regarding the obligation of qada. Fath al-Qadir holds that both are invalidated by menstruation and both become binding upon commencement, so qada is required for both.

Key Terms
طَهُرَتْ (ṭahurat): She became pure from menstruation (hayd) or postpartum bleeding (nifas).
لزوم بالشروع (luzūm bil-shurūʿ): The act of commencing a voluntary worship makes it obligatory to complete it.
قضاء (qadā'): The making up of a missed or invalidated act of worship, performed at a later time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Her fast is valid because she did not eat anything.

C) Her fast is invalid, and she must make it up (qada) because the fast became binding upon commencement.
D) She must continue fasting for the rest of the day and then make it up later.

Key Takeaway
A fast that is invalid due to menstruation must be made up because the act of commencing it made it binding, regardless of the cause of invalidity.
