Qada for Voluntary Fast vs Prayer Started in Menses
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qada for voluntary ibadat started in menses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the majority Hanafi position that a voluntary act becomes binding upon commencement and evaluate Akhi Jalebi's attempted distinction between fast and prayer.

Core Idea
Voluntary acts become binding upon commencement; qada is required regardless of the act's nature.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi debate about whether a woman who begins a voluntary fast while menstruating must make it up if she becomes pure later. Akhi Jalebi argued that the fast does not require qada because the commencement is flawed, unlike prayer. The majority, including the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, rejects this distinction, reaffirming the principle that any valid commencement creates a binding obligation that must be fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify the general rule: voluntary acts (nafl) become binding upon commencement (al-nfl mutlaqan yalzimu bil-shurūʿ).
This is a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh for acts of worship.
2
Identify Akhi Jalebi's opposing view
He argued that for a fast started in menses, the commencement is 'adjacent to the prohibited' (mujāwir lil-manhī ʿanh), so the act is not protected (ṣawān) and no qada is required.
For prayer, he claimed the commencement itself is valid because the prohibition is on the performance (adā') not the start.
3
Analyze the rationale behind the distinction
Understand Jalebi's reasoning: fasting while menstruating is itself prohibited, so the woman is 'similar to the prohibited' (mutashabbiha bil-manhī ʿanh); thus no obligation to preserve. Prayer start is not tied to prostration, so it is valid.
He uses the oath example: if a woman swears not to fast, she breaks the oath by starting to fast. For prayer, the oath is not broken until she prostrates.
4
Examine the author's critique
The author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah points out that this distinction contradicts the settled principle that any invalidator nullifies the voluntary act entirely, and the obligation upon the conscience is not cleared except by performance or release.
The author cites 'Al-Saʿayah' for further support, implying Jalebi's view is weak.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: if a woman starts a voluntary fast while menstruating and becomes pure, the majority says she must make it up; Jalebi says she does not.
The author implies the majority view (qada required) is the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه أخي جلبي في ذخيرة العقبى الفرق: «بأنّ التي شرعت الصوم حائضاً لا يجب عليها صونُ ما أدت ؛ لأنها بمجرد الشروع فيه تصير مشابهة للمنهي . عنه ؛ ولذا تسمّى صائمة بالشروع في الجزء الأول، ألا ترى أنه يحنث به إذا حلفت أن لا تصوم، فلما لم يجب عليها صون المؤدَّى بمباشرة المنهي عنه لم يجب عليها القضاء ؛ لأنَّ وجوبه مبني على وجوب الأداء بخلاف الشروع في الصلاة. فإنّها وإن شرعت فيها حائضاً بناءً على أنّ المفسد في أوله وآخره سواء، لكنَّ الشروع فيها ليس بمجاور للمنهي عنه ؛ لأنَّ التحريم عقد على أدائها، وليس بأداء ؛ لأنّ أداءها لا يكون إلا بالتقييد بالسجدة ؛ ولهذا لا يحنث به من حلف أن لا يصلِّي ما لم يقيد بالسجدة، فلما كان الشروع صحيحاً هاهنا وجب عليها صون المؤدّى فيجب القضاء». انتهى (۲) ولا يخفى عليك أنّ هذا كله مع ما فيه على ما سيطلع عليه من يطالع «السعاية» لا يدفعُ الإشكال، وهو أنه قد تقرّر في الأول أن النفل مطلقاً يلزم بالشروع، وأنّ طَرَيان مفسد له مطلقاً صوماً كان أو صلاة ، وإنّ الواجب في الذمة سواء كان بالشروع المفسد أو بغيره لا تبرأ عنه الذمة إلا بالأداء أو الإبراء، فبناءً على هذا الفرق مشكل.
And the explanation of Akhi Jalebi in 'Dhakirat al-ʿUqba' regarding the difference: 'Because the woman who began the fast while menstruating is not required to preserve what she performed; because by merely starting it she becomes similar to one who is prohibited; therefore she is called a faster by commencement in the first part. Do you not see that she would break her oath if she swore not to fast? So when she is not required to preserve the performed act due to engaging in the prohibited act, she is not required to make it up, because its obligation is based on the obligation of performance, unlike the commencement of prayer. For even though she started it while menstruating, based on the principle that the invalidator at the beginning and end is the same, however the commencement in prayer is not adjacent to the prohibited act; because the prohibition is upon the performance of it, not the commencement; because its performance is only by tying it to the prostration; therefore one who swears not to pray does not break the oath unless he ties it to prostration. So when the commencement is valid here, it becomes obligatory to preserve the performed act, so qada is obligatory.' End. (2) And it is not hidden from you that all this, with what is in it, according to what will be seen by whoever examines 'Al-Saʿayah', does not remove the problem: that it has been established earlier that voluntary acts in general become binding by commencement, and that the occurrence of an invalidator completely nullifies it, whether it is a fast or prayer, and that the obligation upon the conscience, whether due to commencement or otherwise, is not cleared except by performance or release. So based on this, the distinction is problematic.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi debate. Akhi Jalebi in Dhakirat al-ʿUqba attempts to differentiate between starting a voluntary fast and a voluntary prayer while menstruating. He argues that for fasting, the woman resembles the prohibited (i.e., she should not be fasting at all) so her commencement is flawed; thus she is not required to preserve the fast and no qada is due. For prayer, the commencement itself is valid (because salah is only considered performed when tied to prostration), so she must preserve it, and if invalidated, qada is required. However, the author (of ʿUmdat al-Riʿayah) criticizes this distinction, stating that it does not solve the problem because the principle is that any voluntary act becomes binding upon commencement, and once invalidated, qada is necessary. Therefore, the distinction remains problematic.

Key Terms
ذخيرة العقبى (Dhakīrat al-ʿUqbā): A Hanafi fiqh work by Akhi Jalebi (likely a Turkish scholar).
صون المؤدى (ṣawn al-muʾaddā): Preservation of the performed act; i.e., the obligation to protect the validity of the act once started.
مشابهة للمنهي عنه (mutashābiha lil-manhī ʿanh): Resembling the prohibited; used by Jalebi to describe the woman's state when she starts a fast while in menses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, why is Akhi Jalebi's distinction between fast and prayer problematic?
A) Because it contradicts the principle that voluntary acts become binding upon commencement.
B) Because it assumes both acts have the same ruling.
C) Because it ignores the difference between obligatory and voluntary acts.
D) Because it is based on a weak hadith.

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, the majority position is that any voluntary act becomes binding upon commencement, so qada is required for both a fast and a prayer started in menses, despite Akhi Jalebi's attempted distinction.

Validity of Fast When Menstruation Ends at Night on the Tenth Day
Ruling that a woman whose period ends at ten days may fast the next day even if only a moment of night remains

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a woman's fast when her menstruation ends exactly at ten days and she becomes pure at night, and identify the condition of purity for fasting.

Core Idea
If a woman becomes pure at night on the tenth day, her fast the next day is valid — even if only a moment remains — because ten days is the maximum hayd.

This lesson addresses a specific but common scenario in Hanafi fiqh: a woman whose menstrual period ends exactly at the maximum duration of ten days. The ruling clarifies that she may fast the following day as long as she is pure at the time of intention, even if only a brief moment of the night remains. The passage also touches on the general principle that purity from menses is a condition for the validity of acts like fasting and entering the mosque.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The case is a woman whose hayd (menstruation) ends exactly at ten days — the maximum duration in the Hanafi school. She becomes pure (tahir) at night.
2
Understand the condition for fasting
For the fast to be valid, she must be pure from hayd and nifas at the time of the intention (which is made at night) and throughout the day. The text emphasizes that even if only a 'lamḥah' (glance/brief moment) of the night remains, it suffices.
Lamḥah means a very short duration – the Hanafis do not require a full night of purity.
3
Apply the principle of maximum duration
Since ten days is the maximum hayd period, her purity after ten days is definitive. Thus, there is no doubt that the menses has ended, and she may validly fast.
If the blood had stopped before ten days but then returned, different rules apply.
4
Note the indivisibility of worship
The text states that fasting is a single indivisible worship ('ṣawm ʿibādah wāḥidah lā tujazzā''). This means that if an invalidator occurs at the very beginning (e.g., menses at dawn), it invalidates the entire fast. Here, she is pure from before Fajr, so the fast is sound.
This principle is general: an incompatibility at the start affects the whole act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ . وإن طَهُرَتْ في الليل لعشرة أيام يصحُ صومُ هذا اليوم" ، وإن كان الباقي من الليل لمحة. (1) (ودخول المسجد) فقدهما شرط لصحته ، والصومُ عبادة واحدة لا تجزأ، فإذا وجد المنافي في أوله تحقق حكمه في باقيه.
2. And if she becomes pure at night for ten days, the fast of this day is valid, even if only a glance of the night remains. (1) (And entering the mosque) Their absence is a condition for its validity, and fasting is a single indivisible worship; so if an incompatible [element] occurs at its beginning, its ruling is realized in the remainder.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for a woman whose period ends exactly at ten days (the maximum duration for hayd). She becomes pure at night; the fast of the following day is valid, even if only a brief moment of the night remains. This is because her menses is definitively complete after ten days. The text also mentions that the absence of menses and postpartum bleeding is a condition for the validity of entering the mosque (and likely for prayer and fasting). The statement 'fasting is a single indivisible worship' explains why, if incompatibility occurs at the beginning (like menses at dawn), the entire fast is invalidated.

Key Terms
لمحة (lamḥah): A very brief moment, a glance.
حيض (ḥayḍ): Menstrual blood.
طهر (ṭuhr): Purity, state of being free from menses or postpartum bleeding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A woman whose hayd ends at ten days may fast the next day even if only a brief moment of the night remains, because purity at the time of intention suffices.

6 min
Ruling on purity for less than ten days: fast valid with ghusl time
Ruling that fast is valid only if sufficient night time remains

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a woman's fast is valid after her menses stops before completing ten days, and identify that janabah does not invalidate fasting.

Core Idea
A fast is valid even if ghusl is delayed as long as purity occurred with enough night time for ghusl.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh: a woman whose menstrual bleeding ends before the full ten-day maximum. The validity of her fast the next day depends on whether the remaining night time allows for ghusl. Understanding this ruling clarifies the intersection of menstruation, ritual purity, and fasting obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is fasting the next day after becoming pure. The ruling is that the fast is valid (sahih) under certain conditions.
The text states 'يصح الصوم' (the fast is valid).
2
List the conditions for validity
Two conditions: (1) purity occurs before Fajr, (2) the remaining night time is sufficient to perform ghusl and the opening takbir (tahrimah).
The condition of tahrimah indicates that the woman must have enough time for both, but it serves as evidence that bleeding has stopped.
3
Note the exception for delayed ghusl
If she does not perform ghusl at night, the fast remains valid. Janabah (major impurity) does not invalidate fasting.
The text explicitly says: 'وإن لم تغتسل في الليل لا يبطل صومها'.
4
Understand the rationale
The requirement of time for ghusl and tahrimah is to confirm that the bleeding has definitively ended, since hayd can last up to ten days. If there is insufficient time, it is not certain that the impurity has ceased.
This is a precautionary measure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣ . وإن طَهُرَتْ لأقل من عشرة يصحُ الصَّومُ إن كان الباقي من الليل مقدار ما يسعُ الغُسل والتحريمة ) ، وإن لم تغتسل في الليل لا يبطل صومها
3. And if she becomes pure for less than ten [days], the fast is valid if the remainder of the night is sufficient for ghusl and the takbir tahrimah. And if she does not perform ghusl at night, her fast is not invalidated.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This point addresses a woman whose menstrual bleeding stops before completing ten days. Since hayd can last up to ten days, if it stops earlier, it is not certain that the bleeding will not resume. Therefore, the fast of the next day is valid only if there is enough time remaining in the night to perform ghusl (ritual purification) and the takbir tahrimah (the opening Allahu Akbar of prayer) — indicating that the bleeding has definitively ended. If she does not perform ghusl until after Fajr, the fast remains valid because janabah (state of major impurity) does not invalidate fasting. The mention of tahrimah here is a reference to prayer; in the context of fasting, some scholars consider ghusl sufficient without tahrimah.

Key Terms
التحريمة (al-taḥrīmah): The opening takbir (Allahu Akbar) that begins the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman's menses stops on the 8th day, and there is just enough night left for ghusl but she does not perform it. What is the ruling on her fast the next day?
A) The fast is invalid because she did not perform ghusl.
B) The fast is valid and she does not need to make it up.
C) The fast is valid if she performs ghusl after Fajr.
D) The fast is invalid because less than ten days means impurity continues.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a woman's fast after her menses ends before ten days depends on the remaining night time being sufficient for ghusl, but delaying ghusl does not invalidate the fast.

The Prohibition of a Menstruating Woman Entering the Mosque
Ruling that menstruating woman is prohibited from entering mosque with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a menstruating woman entering the mosque, identify the hadith evidence, and explain its application.

Core Idea
A menstruating woman is absolutely prohibited from entering the mosque based on the Prophet's explicit statement.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah establishes a clear prohibition for a menstruating woman (and a junub) to enter the mosque. The ruling is derived directly from a hadith cited by the commentator. Understanding this ruling is essential for maintaining the sanctity of the mosque and observing ritual purity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act under discussion is a menstruating woman entering the mosque. The ruling is that it is prohibited (haram). The text uses the phrase 'يمنع الحيض دخول المسجد' meaning 'menstruation prevents entering the mosque.'
The prohibition also applies to a junub (person in major impurity) and to postpartum bleeding (nifas).
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence is the hadith of the Prophet ﷺ: 'إنّي لا أحلّ المسجد لجنب ولا حائض' — 'Indeed I do not make the mosque lawful for a junub or a menstruating woman.'
This is a direct textual proof (dalil naqli).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'لا أحلّ' (I do not make lawful) indicates a definitive prohibition. The explicit mention of 'ha'id' (menstruating woman) shows that the ruling is specific to her state.
In Hanafi usul, such explicit prohibition indicates haram unless there is a counter-indication.
4
Note the scope
The prohibition applies to entering the mosque voluntarily. It does not address cases of dire necessity (e.g., escaping danger) which may be treated separately by later scholars.
The commentary also includes a note about tahrimah not being considered in fasting and about the 11th day, which are separate issues not directly related to the main ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخول المسجد) عطف على قوله : «الصلاة»، وكذا ما يأتي بعده : أي يمنع الحيض دخول المسجد ؛ لحديث: «إنّي لا أحلّ المسجد لجنب ولا حائض». (1) , صحح صاحب الدر المختار (۱ : ۱۹۷) أنه لا تعتبر التحريمة في الصوم. (٢) أي اليوم الحادي عشر ; لأنها قد أتمت مدة حيضها بيقين وإن لم يبق وقت من الليل يكفي للغسل والتحريمة.

(1) The author of Al-Durr al-Mukhtar (1:197) corrected that the tahrimah is not considered in fasting. (2) i.e., the eleventh day; because she has definitely completed her menstruation period even if there remains no time of night sufficient for ghusl and tahrimah.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage states that menstruation (hayd) and postpartum bleeding (nifas) prohibit entering the mosque. This is based on the prophetic hadith: 'I do not make the mosque lawful for a junub or a menstruating woman.' The text also clarifies two points: first, an annotation by the author of Al-Durr al-Mukhtar (al-Haskafi) that the takbir tahrimah (opening of prayer) is not required for the validity of fasting; second, an explanation that when the text says 'pure for ten days' it means the eleventh day, because after ten complete days of bleeding, the menstruation is definitely over even if there is no time left in the night for ghusl.

Key Terms
حائض (ha'id): A woman experiencing menstruation (hayd).
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity (janabah) requiring ghusl due to sexual intercourse or seminal emission.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding following childbirth.
حيض (hayd): The normal monthly menstrual discharge that a woman experiences for a minimum of 3 and maximum of 10 days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The hadith: 'I do not make the mosque lawful for a junub or a menstruating woman.'
C) Analogy to prayer.
D) The Quranic verse about ritual purity.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of a menstruating woman entering the mosque is based on a clear hadith and is a definitive ruling in the Hanafi school.

Fasting After Menstruation Ends at Night: Conditions for Validity
Ruling on validity of fast when woman becomes pure at night

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the validity of a fast when a woman becomes clean from menstruation at night, and identify the relied-upon position regarding the takbīr.

Core Idea
The fast is valid if she has time for ghusl before dawn; the takbīr is not required in the muftā bihī view.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in fasting: a woman whose menstruation ends during the night before Fajr. The key question is whether her fast is valid and what conditions must be met. The Ḥanafī school has an internal disagreement about whether she must have time for both ghusl and the takbīr (opening of prayer) or only ghusl. We will explore the ruling, its proof, and the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: the fast of a woman who becomes clean from menstruation during the night. The ruling is that the fast is valid (sahīḥ) and also obligatory (wājib) if it is a day of Ramadan or a specified vow.
The text uses 'ṣaḥḥa' (valid) and 'wajaba' (obligatory) depending on context.
2
Locate the condition of time
The passage specifies that if she becomes clean after ten days (i.e., on the eleventh day), the fast is valid even if only a moment remains of the night. If she becomes clean for less than ten days, the remaining night time must be sufficient for ghusl (and originally also for the takbīr).
Ten days is the maximum duration of ḥayḍ; after ten days it is istiḥāḍah.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on takbīr
The early position, according to Abū Ḥanīfa and the school, considered that the remaining time must also allow for the takbīr (opening takbīr of prayer). However, later scholars like al-Ḥaskafī ruled that the takbīr is not a condition for fasting; only ghusl is required.
The commentator explicitly states that al-Ḥaskafī verified this in al-Durr al-Mukhtār.
4
Determine the muftā bihī
The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is that only the time for ghusl is necessary; the takbīr is not considered. This is the view adopted for fatwa.
This reflects the principle that janābah does not invalidate fasting once the impediment (menstruation) has ended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[1] قوله: لكن يجب عليها؛ لحرمة رمضان كما يجب على مسافر أقام بعد نصف النهار، ومجنون أفاق، ومريض صح، وصبي بلغ، وكافر أسلم، وسيتضح تفصيل هذه المسائل في موضعه إن شاء الله تعالى. (2) أي اليوم الحادي عشر؛ لأنها قد أتمت مدة حيضها بيقين وإن لم يبق وقت من الليل يكفي للغسل والتحريمة.
But she must refrain (from eating, etc.). And if she becomes clean at night for ten days, her fast on this day is valid, even if the remainder of the night is just a moment. And if she becomes clean for less than ten days, the fast is valid if the remainder of the night is enough for performing ghusl and the takbīr. And if she did not perform ghusl during the night, her fast is not invalidated. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding the validity of a woman's fast when her menstruation ends during the night. The base matn likely stated that a menstruating woman must refrain from fasting and then resume. The commentator explains that if she becomes clean at night, she must refrain from eating etc. for the remainder of the night out of respect for Ramadan. The key condition for validity is that the remaining time before dawn must be sufficient for ghusl (major ablution). If she becomes clean after ten days, the fast is valid even if only a moment remains. If less than ten days, the time must allow for both ghusl and the takbīr (opening of prayer time), though later scholars like al-Haskafī ruled that the takbīr is not a condition. This reflects the Hanafi principle that ghusl itself removes the impediment of menstruation, and the fast is valid if she performed ghusl after dawn. The text also compares her obligation to that of a traveller settling, a madman regaining sanity, etc.

Key Terms
الغسل (al-ghusl): Major ritual ablution required to remove the state of janābah or after menstruation.
التحريمة (al-taḥrīmah): The opening takbīr (saying 'Allāhu Akbar') that begins the prayer. Here it is used as a measure of time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The fast of a woman who becomes clean from menstruation at night is valid if she has sufficient time for ghusl before dawn; the takbīr is not a condition in the relied-upon position.

Prohibition of Menstruating Woman from Entering Mosque and Performing Tawaf
Expansion on the prohibition of mosque entry and tawaf with intra-Hanafi analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the prohibition of a menstruating woman from entering the mosque and performing tawaf, identify the primary evidence, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the underlying cause.

Core Idea
Tawaf requires purity regardless of location; the prohibition is not merely due to the mosque.

This passage clarifies that menstruation prevents both entering the mosque and performing tawaf. The primary proof is a hadith explicitly prohibiting entry for junub and menstruating women. However, there is a key intra-Hanafi discussion: is tawaf prohibited only because it is performed inside the mosque, or because tawaf itself requires purification? The correct view, as per Fath al-Qadir, is the latter, which has practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Prohibitions
The matn mentions two separate prohibitions: entering the mosque and performing tawaf. Understand that they are distinct rulings, though related.
Entering mosque is prohibited by hadith; tawaf is additionally mentioned to avoid misunderstanding about other Hajj rites.
2
Locate the Primary Dalil
The hadith 'I do not permit the mosque to a person in janabah nor to a menstruating woman' is the core evidence. It is reported by Abu Dawood and Ibn Majah. Note its absolute wording covers all types of entry.
The hadith is ahad but clear in meaning (dalalah qati).
3
Analyze the First Reasoning (al-Hidayah)
Some Hanafi scholars, like the author of al-Hidayah, reasoned that tawaf is prohibited because it is performed in the mosque. Since the hadith forbids entry, and tawaf is inside, it falls under that prohibition.
This reasoning is based on the location of tawaf.
4
Understand the Objection and Reply
Objection: mention of tawaf is redundant after forbidding entry. Reply: it was mentioned separately to prevent the misconception that all Hajj rites are permissible for a menstruating woman.
The reply acknowledges the potential for misunderstanding.
5
Recognize the Correct Reasoning
The mufta bihi view (from Fath al-Qadir) states that the cause is not the mosque but that tawaf itself requires purification. Even if tawaf is performed outside the mosque, it is still prohibited for a menstruating woman.
Practical consequence: if the mosque is destroyed or tawaf is done outside, prohibition remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ودخول المسجد ; عطف على قوله : «الصلاة»، وكذا ما يأتي بعده : أي يمنع الحيض دخول المسجد ؛ لحديث: «إنّي لا أحلّ المسجد لجنب ولا حائض» والطواف والطواف )؛ لكونه يُفْعَلُ في المسجد، فإن طافت مع هذا تحللت. أخرجه أبو داود وغيره، وفي رواية ابن ماجة: «إنّ المسجد لا يحل لجنب ولا حائض». وفي الإطلاق إشارة إلى أنّ الدخول مطلقاً ممنوع، سواء كان على سبيل المكث أو على سبيل العبور، وإلى أنّ المساجد كلّها سواء في هذا الحكم، ولكن يستثنى منه مصلّى العيد ومصلى الجنازة، فإنّه ليس لهما حكم المسجد في حرمة دخول الجنب، كما فصله في «البحر الرائق» وغيره. [1] قوله : والطواف ; سواء كان فرضاً؛ كطواف الزيارة للحج أو نفلاً. [2] قوله : لكونه؛ هكذا علّله جمعٌ منهم صاحب «الهداية»، وبناءً عليه أورد عليهم بأنه لا حاجة إلى ذكر الطواف بعد ذكر ممانعة دخول المسجد. وأجيب عنه : بأنه ذكر على حدة؛ لئلا يتوهم أن جميع أركان الحج ومناسكه كالوقوف بعرفة وبالمزدلفة ويمنى وغيرها جائزة للجنب والحائض، فكذا يجوز الطواف لها، فلدفع هذا الوهم صرحوا بمنعه. والحق على ما يفهم من فتح القدير وغيره : أنَّ هذا التعليل قاصر، وأنه ليست العلة في حرمة الطواف كونه يفعل في المسجد، بل الطهارة مشروطة لنفس الطواف، حتى لو لم يكن ثمة مسجد كما كان في زمان سيدنا إبراهيم على نبينا وعليه الصلاة والتسليم، وكما لو هدم المسجد الحرام، أو طاف طائف خارج المسجد، ودار حول الكعبة من خارج المسجد الحرام لم يجز أيضاً. [3] قوله : فإن طافت؛ أي فإن طافت الحائض مع هذا؛ أي مع حرمته أثمت وتحللت من التحلل; أي خرجت من إحرامها بطواف الزيارة، لكن يجب عليها ذبح بدنة كفارة له. (1) في سنن ابن ماجة (1): (212)، والمعجم الكبير (23: 373)، وغيرها. (2) البحر الرائق (1): (205)، وأخرج أيضاً مسجد المدرسة والرباط. (3) الهداية (1): 166. (4) فتح القدير (1: 166).
The author's saying: 'and entering the mosque' is conjoined to his saying: 'prayer', and likewise what follows: meaning menstruation prevents entering the mosque, due to the hadith: 'I do not permit the mosque to a person in janabah nor to a menstruating woman', and tawaf, because it is performed in the mosque. So if she performs tawaf despite this, she becomes free from her ihram. Abu Dawood and others have reported it. In the narration of Ibn Majah: 'The mosque is not permitted to a person in janabah nor to a menstruating woman.' The absolute wording indicates that entry is absolutely prohibited, whether for staying or passing through, and that all mosques are the same in this ruling. However, an exception is made for the prayer ground of the Eid and the funeral prayer ground, for they do not have the ruling of a mosque regarding the prohibition of entering for a person in janabah, as detailed in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. [1] The author's saying: 'and tawaf' – whether obligatory, like the tawaf of visitation for Hajj, or supererogatory. [2] The author's saying: 'because it' – this is the reasoning of many, including the author of al-Hidayah. Based on this, it was objected: there is no need to mention tawaf after mentioning the prohibition of entering the mosque. The answer: it was mentioned separately to prevent misunderstanding that all Hajj rites are permissible for a junub or menstruating woman. The truth, as understood from Fath al-Qadir, is that the reasoning is deficient; the cause is not that tawaf is in the mosque, but that purification is required for tawaf itself. Even if there were no mosque, or if tawaf is performed outside, it would not be valid. [3] The author's saying: 'so if she performs tawaf' – meaning if the menstruating woman performs tawaf despite the prohibition, she commits a sin and becomes free from her ihram, but she must sacrifice a female camel as expiation.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage explains the prohibition of a menstruating woman from entering the mosque and performing tawaf. The commentator clarifies that the matn's mention of 'and entering the mosque' is linked to the earlier mention of 'prayer'. The primary evidence is the hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) said he does not permit the mosque to a junub or a menstruating woman. This prohibition includes all types of entry, whether for staying or just passing through, and applies to all mosques equally. An exception is made for the Eid prayer ground and funeral prayer ground, which do not have the same ruling. The commentator then discusses the reason for prohibiting tawaf: some scholars, like the author of al-Hidayah, reasoned that because tawaf is performed in the mosque, it is automatically prohibited. Objection is raised that mentioning tawaf separately is redundant. The reply is that it was mentioned to prevent a misconception that all other Hajj rites could be done by a junub or menstruating woman. However, the correct view is that tawaf itself requires purification, regardless of it being in the mosque. If a menstruating woman performs tawaf, she commits a sin, but her tawaf counts to release her from ihram, and she must offer a female camel as expiation. This reflects an intra-Hanafi discussion on the underlying cause (illah) of the prohibition.

Key Terms
الطواف (al-ṭawāf): Circumambulation of the Kaaba, an essential rite of Hajj and Umrah.
البدنة (al-badanah): A female camel or cow sacrificed as expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because tawaf itself requires a state of purity, regardless of location.
C) Because she is in a state of janabah.
D) Because she cannot walk properly.

Key Takeaway
A menstruating woman is prohibited from entering the mosque and performing tawaf; tawaf itself requires purity, and if performed despite prohibition, she releases from ihram but must sacrifice a badanah.

Permissible Intimacy with a Menstruating Woman
Commentary on the ruling for intimacy with a menstruating woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on permissible physical intimacy with a menstruating woman and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Intimacy above the izaar is permissible; below the izaar, only the private part is prohibited according to Imam Muhammad.

In this passage, the commentator expands the ruling on what is permissible for a husband regarding intimate contact with his menstruating wife. The key terms 'under the izaar' and 'above the izaar' are defined, and a hadith-based dispute among the Hanafi imams is presented. You will learn the exact limits of what is allowed and the evidence for each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by stating 'And enjoyment of what is under the izaar' — note that this is the issue being ruled upon. Determine the overall hukm level: mubah (permissible) with restrictions.
The commentator clarifies that 'under the izaar' means from navel to knee.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The first hadith cited is 'You may have what is above the izaar' (lak ma fawq al-izar). Analyze its implication: it explicitly permits what is above the izaar, but by implication restricts what is below it.
This hadith is used by the majority Hanafi view to limit intimacy to above the izaar.
3
Examine the secondary dalil and Muhammad's view
The second hadith 'Do everything except intercourse' is presented as 'easier.' Understand how Imam Muhammad uses this to permit everything under the izaar except the private part.
This hadith is more permissive; Muhammad restricts only actual intercourse.
4
Define the key terms
The commentary defines 'mubasharah' and 'shiar al-dam'. Note that 'shiar al-dam' refers specifically to the private part (farj), which is the only prohibited area according to Muhammad.
Mubasharah fahishah (shameless direct contact) nullifies wudu, but that is a separate ruling.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference between the two views: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf allow only above the izaar; Muhammad allows everything except the private part. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the more restrictive one.
Check the footnote references to later works like al-Durr al-Mukhtar for tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستمتاع ما تحت الإزار ولا تقرأ هي للقرآن واستمتاع ما تحت الإزار (كالمباشرة) والتفخيذ. وتحل القبلة، وملامسة ما فوق الإزار، وعند محمد: يتقي (۳) شعار الدم، أي موضع الفرج فقط. (ولا تقرأ هي للقرآن) وبالجملة الطواف في حالة الحيض وإن كان ممنوعاً لحديث عائشة وقد حاضت في الإحرام في سفر حجة الوداع، قال لها النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: افعلي ما يفعل الحاج، غير أن لا تطوفي بالبيت، لكن لو صدر ذلك من أحد يترتب حكمه عليه. [١] قوله: واستمتاع ما تحت الإزار؛ يعني من تحت السرة إلى الركبة؛ لحديث: لك ما فوق الإزار (۲)، قاله النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لمن سأل عما يحل له من الحائض، أخرجه أحمد وأبو داود وابن ماجه وغيرهم. وقد جاء ما هو أيسر من هذا، وهو حديث: «اصنعوا كل شيء - أي بالحائض - إلا النكاح (۳): أي الجماع» أخرجه الترمذي والنسائي وابن ماجة وغيره، وبناءً عليه ذهب محمد رحمه الله أنه يحرم الجماع فقط، وإن شئت زيادة التفصيل في هذا المبحث فارجع إلى حاشيتي لـ«موطأ الإمام محمد» المسماة بـ«التعليق الممجد». [٢] قوله: كالمباشرة؛ هو ما يكون لمس الجلد بالجلد كالمعانقة، ومنه المباشرة الفاحشة، وقد مر ذكرها في «بحث نواقض الوضوء». (۳) قوله: يتقي؛ بصيغة المعروف أو المجهول؛ أي يتجنب المستمتع شعار الدم. هو بالكسر في اللغة بمعنى العلامة، وبمعنى الثوب الذي يلي الجسد، والمراد به موضع الدم والكرسف، وهو الفرج. [٤] قوله: ولا تقرأ؛ أي يحرم على الحائض أن تقرأ القرآن، وكذا على الجنب كجنب ونفساء، سواء كان آية أو ما دونها عند الكرخي، وهو المختار، وعند الطحاوي: يحل ما دون الآية، هذا إذا قصدت القراءة بالوطء أو بالاحتلام، وكذا النفساء؛ لحديث: «لا تقرأ الحائض ولا الجنب شيئاً من القرآن» أخرجه الترمذي والدارقطني، وورد عند أصحاب السنن الأربعة وغيرهم: كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يحجبه عن القرآن شيء ليس الجنابة. [1] قوله: نفساء؛ بضم النون، وفتح الفاء التي بها نفاس، قال البرجندي: حكم النفاس حكم الحيض في جميع الأمور إلا أن العدة لا تنقضي به، ذكره في «الخزانة» فلا وجه لتخصيص النفساء بعدم القراءة؛ إذ يوهم أنه يجوز لها الأمور الأخر. [٢] قوله: يحل ما دون الآية؛ بناء على أن المفروض في الصلاة المفسر به قوله: فَأَقْرَهُوا مَا تَبَسَّرَ مِنَ الْقُرْمَانِ) (المزمل: 20)، هو مقدار ثلاث آيات قصار أو آية فما دون. 

الهوامش:
(۱) في صحيح البخاري (۱ : ۱۱۷)، وصحيح مسلم رقم (۱۲۰)، وغيرها.
(۲) في سنن أبي داود (۱ : ۵۵)، والسنن الصغرى (۱ : ۱۲۳)، وسنن الدارمي (۱ : ۲۵۹)، وغيرها، قال القاري في فتح باب العناية (۱ : ۲۱۴): حسنه البعض، وقال العراقي: ينبغي أن يكون صحيحاً.
(۳) بلفظ: «افعلوا كل شيء إلا الجماع» في سنن النسائي الكبرى (۵ : ۳۴۵)، وسنن ابن ماجة (۱ : ۲۱۱)، وغيرها.
(1) واختاره صاحب الدر المختار (۱ : ۱۱۶)، والملتقى (ص ٤)، والمراقي (ص ۱۷۸)، والاختيار (۱ : ۲۱)، والكنز (ص ۷)، وغيرها.
(2) قال الطحاوي في مختصره (ص ۱۸)، وشرح معاني الآثار (۱ : ۹۰): ولا يقرأ الجنب ولا الحائض الآية التامة.
(3) وهو رواية ابن سماعة عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله، ووجهه: أنه إن قرأ ما دون الآية لا يعد بها قارئاً. ورجحه صاحب الفتح (۱ : ۱۴۸).
(4) في سنن الترمذي (۱ : ۲۳۶)، وسنن البيهقي الكبير (۱ : ۳۰۹)، وغيرها.
(5) في صحيح ابن خزيمة (۱ : ۱۰۴)، وصحيح ابن حبان (۱ : ۵۱۰)، وسنن الترمذي (۱ : ۲۷۳)، وقال: حسن صحيح، ومصنف ابن أبي شيبة (۱ : ۹۹)، ومسند أحمد (۱ : ۸۳)، ومسند أبي يعلى (۱ : ۴۵۹)، وغيرها. وأيضاً عن علي رضي الله عنه قال: رأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم توضأ فقرأ آياً من القرآن، ثم قال هكذا لمن ليس بجنب، فأما الجنب فلا ولا آية. (في مسند أبي يعلى ۱ : ۳۰۰)، والأحاديث المختارة (۲ : ۲۴٤)، وقال إسناده صحيح، وقال الهيثمي في مجمع الزوائد (۱ : ۲۷۶): رجاله موثقون. وأيضاً عن عبد الله بن رواحة رضي الله عنه: إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى أن يقرأ أحدنا القرآن وهو جنب. (في سنن الدارقطني (۱ : ۱۲۰)، وقال: إسناده صالح.
(6) المزمل: من الآية ۲۰.
And enjoyment of what is under the izaar, and she does not recite Quran. And enjoyment of what is under the izaar (such as direct contact) and thighing. And kissing is permissible, and touching above the izaar. And according to Muhammad, he should avoid the mark of blood, meaning the place of the private part only. (And she does not recite Quran). And in summary, tawaf during menstruation is prohibited because of the hadith of Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, when she menstruated during ihram in the journey of the Farewell Pilgrimage; the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to her: 'Do what the pilgrim does, except do not circumambulate the House.' But if someone does that, the ruling applies to him. [1] His statement: 'And enjoyment of what is under the izaar' means from below the navel to the knee; due to the hadith: 'You may have what is above the izaar' (2), said by the Prophet, peace be upon him, to the one who asked what is permissible for him from the menstruating woman, narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, and others. And came what is easier than this, the hadith: 'Do everything - meaning with the menstruating woman - except marriage (3): i.e., intercourse', narrated by al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, and others. Based on this, Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, held that only intercourse is prohibited. And if you wish for more detail on this topic, refer to my gloss on the Muwatta of Imam Muhammad, called 'al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad'. [2] His statement: 'such as direct contact' is skin-to-skin touching like embracing, and from it is shameless direct contact, which was mentioned in the chapter on nullifiers of wudu. (3) His statement: 'he should avoid' in the active or passive form means the one enjoying should avoid the mark of blood. In language, it means sign, and also the garment that touches the body; what is meant is the place of blood and cotton, i.e., the private part. [4] His statement: 'And she does not recite' means it is prohibited for the menstruating woman to recite Quran, and similarly for the junub, like a junub and a woman in nifas, whether it is a whole verse or less according to al-Karkhi, which is the chosen view. And according to al-Tahawi, less than a verse is permissible, this is when she intends recitation due to intercourse or wet dream, and likewise the woman in nifas. Due to the hadith: 'The menstruating woman and the junub should not recite anything of the Quran' narrated by al-Tirmidhi and al-Daraqutni. And it is narrated by the authors of the four Sunan and others: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was not prevented from Quran by anything except janabah.' [1] His statement: 'nifas' with damma on the nun and fatha on the fa; al-Birjandi said: 'The ruling of nifas is like the ruling of menstruation in all matters except that the waiting period does not elapse with it,' mentioned in al-Khazanah, so there is no reason to single out the woman in nifas for not reciting, as it implies that other matters are permissible for her. [2] His statement: 'less than a verse is permissible' is based on that which is prescribed in prayer, interpreted by His statement: 'So recite what is easy of the Quran' (al-Muzzammil 20), which is the quantity of three short verses or one verse or less. Footnotes: (1) In Sahih al-Bukhari (1:117), Sahih Muslim (no. 120), etc. (2) In Sunan Abi Dawud (1:55), al-Sunan al-Sughra (1:123), Sunan al-Darimi (1:259), etc.; al-Qari in Fath Bab al-Inayah (1:214) said: 'Some declared it hasan'; al-Iraqi said: 'It should be sahih.' (3) With the wording: 'Do everything except intercourse' in Sunan al-Nasa'i al-Kubra (5:345), Sunan Ibn Majah (1:211), etc. (1) Chosen by the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar (1:116), al-Multaqa (p. 4), al-Maraqi (p. 178), al-Ikhtiyar (1:21), al-Kanz (p. 7), etc. (2) Al-Tahawi said in his Mukhtasar (p. 18) and Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar (1:90): 'The junub and the menstruating woman should not recite a complete verse.' (3) It is the narration of Ibn Sama'ah from Abu Hanifah, may Allah have mercy on him; its face is that if one recites less than a verse, he is not considered a reciter. It was preferred by the author of al-Fath (1:148). (4) In Sunan al-Tirmidhi (1:236), Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (1:309), etc. (5) In Sahih Ibn Khuzaymah (1:104), Sahih Ibn Hibban (1:510), Sunan al-Tirmidhi (1:273), and he said: 'Hasan Sahih', Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah (1:99), Musnad Ahmad (1:83), Musnad Abi Ya'la (1:459), etc. Also from Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'I saw the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, perform wudu and recite a verse of Quran, then he said: 'This is for one who is not junub; as for the junub, no, not even a verse.' (in Musnad Abi Ya'la 1:300), and al-Ahadith al-Mukhtarah (2:244), and he said its chain is sahih, and al-Haythami in Majma' al-Zawa'id (1:276) said: 'Its narrators are trustworthy.' Also from Abdullah ibn Rawahah, may Allah be pleased with him: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, forbade any of us to recite Quran while junub.' (in Sunan al-Daraqutni (1:120), and he said: 'Its chain is sound.') (6) al-Muzzammil: from verse 20.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of al-Wiqayah explains two distinct rulings for the menstruating woman: permissible physical intimacy and the prohibition of Quran recitation. The author first clarifies that 'what is under the izaar' refers to the area from the navel to the knee, and this is permissible based on the hadith 'you may have what is above the izaar' – though the wording may seem to indicate the opposite, the intended meaning is that the area covered by the izaar (lower body) is permitted for enjoyment except the immediate genital area. A secondary hadith 'do everything except intercourse' leads Imam Muhammad to restrict prohibition only to actual intercourse. The commentary then defines 'mubasharah' as skin-to-skin contact and 'shiar al-dam' as the private part. The second ruling prohibits the menstruating woman from reciting Quran, with the same applying to the junub and the woman in nifas. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Karkhi holds that any amount of Quran, even less than a verse, is prohibited, and this is the relied-upon position; al-Tahawi permits less than a full verse, reasoning that less than a verse is not considered Quranic recitation. The passage also equates nifas with hayd in all rulings except for the 'iddah, and clarifies that the prohibition is specific to recitation (tilawah), not other forms of dhikr.

Key Terms
ما تحت الإزار (ma tahta al-izar): The area from below the navel to the knee, which is covered by the lower garment (izar).
مباشرة (mubasharah): Skin-to-skin contact, such as embracing; includes shameless direct contact (mubasharah fahishah) which nullifies wudu.
شعار الدم (shi'ar al-dam): The mark of blood, referring to the private part (farj) of the woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) Hanafi position, what is the scope of permissible physical intimacy with a menstruating wife?
A) Only kissing and hugging above the neck.
B) Everything except vaginal intercourse.
C) Above the izaar (navel and above, and legs below knee).
D) No physical contact at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab permits intimacy with a menstruating wife above the izaar, with a stronger view restricting it to above the navel and below the knees, and a weaker view permitting all except intercourse.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
Prohibition of Quran Recitation for Menstruating Women
Commentary on the prohibition of Quran recitation for menstruating women and junub

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Quran recitation for menstruating women and junub, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding reciting less than a full verse.

Core Idea
A menstruating woman or junub is prohibited from reciting any part of the Quran, even less than a verse, according to the strongest Hanafi position.

This passage addresses the prohibition of Quran recitation for a menstruating woman and a junub person. The commentator presents the central hadith evidence and then examines an intra-Hanafi dispute: does the prohibition apply to less than a full verse? You will learn the evidence for each view and the relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its scope
The text states: 'she does not recite the Quran' — this is a clear prohibition (haram). The ruling applies to menstruating woman, junub, and woman in nifas. Identify the hukm level.
The prohibition is for tilawah (recitation), not dhikr or du'a.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith 'The menstruating woman and the junub should not recite anything of the Quran' is cited. Note the phrase 'anything of the Quran' – this is general (amm) and includes any part.
This hadith is from al-Tirmidhi and al-Daraqutni; its chain is considered hasan.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Karkhi holds that the prohibition includes any amount, even less than a verse. Al-Tahawi holds that less than a verse is permissible, because only a complete verse is considered qira'ah.
Al-Karkhi's view is declared 'al-mukhtar' (chosen) by the commentator.
4
Analyse the opposing dalil
Al-Tahawi uses the verse 'So recite what is easy of the Quran' (al-Muzzammil 20) to argue that 'recite' can apply to less than a verse. Also a narration from Ibn Sama'ah from Abu Hanifa permits less than a verse.
This is a weaker transmission (nawadir).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator clearly states that al-Karkhi's view is the chosen one (mukhtar), and later works like al-Durr al-Mukhtar adopt it. Thus the prohibition of any Quranic recitation, even a single word, is the operative fatwa.
The thamarah al-khilaf: a menstruating woman cannot recite even 'Bismillah' as Quranic recitation.

And enjoyment of what is under the izaar, and she does not recite Quran. And enjoyment of what is under the izaar (such as direct contact) and thighing. And kissing is permissible, and touching above the izaar. And according to Muhammad, he should avoid the mark of blood, meaning the place of the private part only. (And she does not recite Quran). And in summary, tawaf during menstruation is prohibited because of the hadith of Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, when she menstruated during ihram in the journey of the Farewell Pilgrimage; the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to her: 'Do what the pilgrim does, except do not circumambulate the House.' But if someone does that, the ruling applies to him. [1] His statement: 'And enjoyment of what is under the izaar' means from below the navel to the knee; due to the hadith: 'You may have what is above the izaar' (2), said by the Prophet, peace be upon him, to the one who asked what is permissible for him from the menstruating woman, narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, and others. And came what is easier than this, the hadith: 'Do everything - meaning with the menstruating woman - except marriage (3): i.e., intercourse', narrated by al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, and others. Based on this, Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, held that only intercourse is prohibited. And if you wish for more detail on this topic, refer to my gloss on the Muwatta of Imam Muhammad, called 'al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad'. [2] His statement: 'such as direct contact' is skin-to-skin touching like embracing, and from it is shameless direct contact, which was mentioned in the chapter on nullifiers of wudu. (3) His statement: 'he should avoid' in the active or passive form means the one enjoying should avoid the mark of blood. In language, it means sign, and also the garment that touches the body; what is meant is the place of blood and cotton, i.e., the private part. [4] His statement: 'And she does not recite' means it is prohibited for the menstruating woman to recite Quran, and similarly for the junub, like a junub and a woman in nifas, whether it is a whole verse or less according to al-Karkhi, which is the chosen view. And according to al-Tahawi, less than a verse is permissible, this is when she intends recitation due to intercourse or wet dream, and likewise the woman in nifas. Due to the hadith: 'The menstruating woman and the junub should not recite anything of the Quran' narrated by al-Tirmidhi and al-Daraqutni. And it is narrated by the authors of the four Sunan and others: 'The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was not prevented from Quran by anything except janabah.' [1] His statement: 'nifas' with damma on the nun and fatha on the fa; al-Birjandi said: 'The ruling of nifas is like the ruling of menstruation in all matters except that the waiting period does not elapse with it,' mentioned in al-Khazanah, so there is no reason to single out the woman in nifas for not reciting, as it implies that other matters are permissible for her. [2] His statement: 'less than a verse is permissible' is based on that which is prescribed in prayer, interpreted by His statement: 'So recite what is easy of the Quran' (al-Muzzammil 20), which is the quantity of three short verses or one verse or less. (in Musnad Abi Ya'la 1:300), and al-Ahadith al-Mukhtarah (2:244), and he said its chain is sahih, and al-Haythami in Majma' al-Zawa'id (1:276) said: 'Its narrators are trustworthy.' Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of al-Wiqayah explains two distinct rulings for the menstruating woman: permissible physical intimacy and the prohibition of Quran recitation. The author first clarifies that 'what is under the izaar' refers to the area from the navel to the knee, and this is permissible based on the hadith 'you may have what is above the izaar' – though the wording may seem to indicate the opposite, the intended meaning is that the area covered by the izaar (lower body) is permitted for enjoyment except the immediate genital area. A secondary hadith 'do everything except intercourse' leads Imam Muhammad to restrict prohibition only to actual intercourse. The commentary then defines 'mubasharah' as skin-to-skin contact and 'shiar al-dam' as the private part. The second ruling prohibits the menstruating woman from reciting Quran, with the same applying to the junub and the woman in nifas. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Karkhi holds that any amount of Quran, even less than a verse, is prohibited, and this is the relied-upon position; al-Tahawi permits less than a full verse, reasoning that less than a verse is not considered Quranic recitation. The passage also equates nifas with hayd in all rulings except for the 'iddah, and clarifies that the prohibition is specific to recitation (tilawah), not other forms of dhikr.

Key Terms
آية (ayah): A verse of the Quran; the dispute centers on whether reciting less than a full verse is considered recitation (qira'ah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (al-mukhtar), what is the ruling on a menstruating woman reciting less than a full verse of the Quran?
A) It is permissible because less than a verse is not considered Quranic recitation.
B) It is prohibited because any amount of Quran is included in the prohibition.
C) It is makruh tanzihi, disliked but not sinful.
D) It is wajib for her to recite to avoid forgetting.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab prohibits a menstruating woman, junub, and woman in nifas from reciting any amount of the Quran with the intention of tilawah.

Prohibition of Quran Recitation for Menstruating Woman and Junub
Ruling with proof and internal ikhtilaf on reciting less than an ayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Quran recitation for a menstruating woman and junub, identify the internal Hanafi dispute regarding less than an ayah, and apply the exception for non-recitational intention.

Core Idea
A menstruating woman and junub are prohibited from reciting the Quran, but words without recitational intent are permissible.

This passage addresses the sensitive issue of Quranic recitation during states of ritual impurity. We will examine the primary ruling, the evidence from the Sunnah, and an important internal Hanafi dispute over reciting less than a full verse. You will also learn the critical distinction between recitational intent and other speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act of reciting the Quran by a menstruating woman, a woman with nifas, and a junub. The ruling is haram (prohibited).
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The hadith: 'لا تقرأ الحائض ولا الجنب شيئاً من القرآن' (Do not recite anything of the Quran). Also additional narrations from Ali and Abdullah ibn Rawahah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The explicit prohibition (nahy) indicates the act is haram according to the Hanafi principle that a prohibition implies impermissibility.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Karkhi holds that even less than an ayah is prohibited (the relied-upon view). Al-Tahawi holds that less than an ayah is permissible. Also, a riwayah from Ibn Sama'ah permits less than an ayah.
5
Analyze the exception for non-recitational intention
If the words are said without the intention of Quranic recitation (e.g., as praise or supplication), it is permissible. The intention changes the ruling.
Examples: saying 'Al-hamdu lillah' as gratitude, or reciting al-Fatihah as a supplication.

[1] His saying: no harm; because the words of the Quran change from being Quranic by differing intention, and that affects it. So if she recites al-Fatihah as a supplication or something that has the meaning of supplication and the like, and does not intend recitation, it is permissible, unlike if she recites Surah Abi Lahab and the like, where the intention of non-Quranic does not affect it.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the prohibition of Quran recitation for a menstruating woman and a person in a state of janabah, along with exceptions and an internal Hanafi dispute. The author begins by stating the established ruling: a menstruating woman (hayd) and a postpartum woman (nufasa') are like a junub in that they are prohibited from reciting the Quran. The relied-upon view (al-mukhtar) according to al-Karkhi is that this applies whether the recitation is a complete ayah or less than an ayah. However, al-Tahawi holds that less than a complete ayah is permissible. The proof is the hadith 'The menstruating woman and the junub should not recite anything of the Quran' and other narrations. The commentary clarifies the meaning of 'nufasa'' and emphasizes that nifas is analogous to hayd in all rulings except the waiting period. Then it explains that 'less than an ayah' relates to the minimal amount recited in prayer according to the verse {So recite what is easy of the Quran}. The passage also mentions that the opinion permitting less than an ayah is attributed to Ibn Sama'ah from Abu Hanifa and is preferred. Additional supporting hadith from Ali and Abdullah ibn Rawahah are cited. Finally, an important exception is given: if the words are said without the intention of Quranic recitation, such as saying 'Al-hamdu lillahi rabbi l-'alamin' as a praise, then it is permissible. The commentary elaborates that intent changes the ruling; reciting al-Fatihah as a supplication is permissible, but reciting Surah Masad (Abi Lahab) with the intent of meaning does not remove the Quranic status.

Key Terms
حائض (ha'id): A woman experiencing menstrual bleeding
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl
آية (ayah): A verse of the Quran; here the dispute concerns the minimum amount that triggers the prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is prohibited because any Quranic words are forbidden.
C) It is permissible only if she says it as a non-recitational prayer.
D) It is makruh tanzihi but not haram.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits menstruating women and junub from reciting the Quran, with the relied-upon view applying to any amount, but words without recitational intent are exempt.

Teaching Quran and Qunut for a Menstruating Woman
Ruling on teaching Quran and qunut for menstruating woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a menstruating woman teaching Quran and reciting the qunut supplication, and explain the methodological differences within the school.

Core Idea
A menstruating woman may teach Quran in broken segments and recite qunut – the prohibition applies only to continuous tilawah.

This lesson examines two specific concessions granted to a menstruating woman: teaching Quran and reciting the qunut supplication. While a woman in her menses is generally prohibited from reciting the Quran, necessity and the nature of the act provide exceptions. We explore the intra-Hanafi differences on the teaching method and the status of the qunut.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
Determine the two acts in question (teaching Quran by spelling, and reciting qunut) and their default permissibility under conditions.
Teaching is permissible if not continuous recitation; qunut is allowed because it is not Quran.
2
Examine the condition for teaching: method of breaking the text
According to al-Karkhi: teach word by word, pausing between words. According to al-Tahawi: teach half an ayah, pause, then the other half. Understand the rationale of avoiding continuous tilawah.
Both methods are valid; the difference is the length of the taught segment.
3
Analyze the dispute on qunut: Is it Quran?
Some scholars dislike qunut for a menstruating woman because they consider it Quranic, citing a narration from Ibn Mas'ud. The relied-upon view is that it is not Quran, hence not disliked.
The majority of Companions held qunut as a supplication, not Quran.
4
Apply the mufta bihi
Teaching is permissible with careful segmentation (either method); qunut is permissible without dislike. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
The author endorses the view in al-Muhit that qunut is not makruh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز لها التهجي بالقرآن والتعليم، والمعلمة إذا حاضت فعند الكَرْخِي تعلم كلمة كلمة، وتقطعُ بين الكلمتين، وعند الطحاوي: نصف آية وتقطع، ثم تُعَلِّمُ النِّصف الآخر. وأما دعاء القنوت، فيكره عند بعض المشايخ، وفي «المحيط»: لا يُكْرَهُ. [2] قوله: والمعلمة؛ أي المرأة التي تعلم الأطفال القرآن. [3] قوله: فيكره؛ بناءً على ما روي عن ابن مسعود: وإن القنوت من القرآن، وكان سورتين أحدهما سورة الخلع، وهي: بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم، اللهم إنا نستعينك إلى قوله: مَن يفجرك، والثانية: سورة الحقد، وهي: بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم اللهم إياك نعبد إلى ملحق، كذا ذكره السيوطي في «الدر المنثور». And it is permissible for her to spell out the Quran and teach it. And the female teacher, if she gets her menses, according to al-Karkhi teaches word by word and pauses between the two words; according to al-Tahawi, half an ayah, then stops, then teaches the other half. As for the qunut supplication, it is disliked according to some scholars, and in al-Muhit: it is not disliked. [2] His saying: and the female teacher; meaning the woman who teaches children the Quran. [3] His saying: it is disliked; based on what is narrated from Ibn Mas'ud that the qunut is from the Quran, and it was two surahs: one is Surat al-Khal' (which is 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim, Allahumma inna nasta'inuka' up to his saying 'man yafjuruk'), and the second is Surat al-Haqd (which is 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim, Allahumma iyyaka na'budu' up to 'mulhaq'). This is mentioned by al-Suyuti in al-Durr al-Manthur. [4] His saying: it is not disliked; based on the fact that it is not from the Quran according to the majority of the Companions, and that is correct. Hence the basmalah is not recited at its beginning, and this matter is detailed in my treatise Ihkam al-Qantarah bi Ahkam al-Basmalah.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage addresses two related rulings for a menstruating woman. First, it is permissible for her to engage in teaching the Quran through spelling (al-tahajji) and instructing children. However, there is a methodological difference: according to al-Karkhi, the female teacher should teach one word at a time and break between words, while according to al-Tahawi, she may teach half an ayah and then pause before the other half. The practical difference is the length of the taught segment. Secondly, the passage deals with the qunut supplication (du'a al-qunut) recited in witr prayer. Some scholars consider it makruh (disliked) for a menstruating woman because they view qunut as part of the Quran, citing a narration from Ibn Mas'ud that the qunut was considered a surah. However, the relied-upon view, as stated in al-Muhit, is that it is not makruh, because qunut is not actually Quranic; the majority of companions did not consider it as such. The author notes that this is the correct view, and the basmalah is not recited before qunut, reinforcing its non-Quranic status. Therefore, a menstruating woman may recite the qunut supplication without prohibition.

Key Terms
التهجي (al-tahajji): Spelling out or pronouncing letters of the Quran without reciting continuous verses, used in teaching.
دعاء القنوت (du'a al-qunut): A supplication recited in the witr prayer; its status as Quranic is disputed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a menstruating woman reciting the qunut supplication in prayer?
A) It is haram because qunut is part of the Quran.
B) It is makruh according to all Hanafi scholars.
C) It is not disliked because qunut is not Quranic.
D) It is mustahab to recite it.

Key Takeaway
A menstruating woman may teach Quran by segmenting the text and may recite the qunut supplication, as these acts do not constitute continuous tilawah.

Rulings for Menstruating Women and Junub on Quran Recitation and Touching
Commentary expanding rulings on Quran for those in major impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on Quran recitation and touching for menstruating women and persons in major impurity, and apply the principle that intention changes the legal nature of recitation.

Core Idea
Intention changes the ruling: Quranic words become dua when recited without intent of tilawah; only intentional tilawah is prohibited.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands the rulings for menstruating women and those in janabah regarding the Quran. It explores key distinctions: between intentional recitation (tilawah) and supplication (du'a), between major and minor impurity, and between Quran and other divine scriptures. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying the rules of taharah in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited act
The passage makes it clear that reciting Quran with the intention of tilawah is prohibited for a menstruating woman and a junub. Touching the mushaf is also prohibited. Identify the act being ruled upon.
2
Understand the role of intention
The same words, if spoken as supplication or thanks without intending tilawah, become permissible. This is a key principle: intention changes the legal nature of the words from Quranic to non-Quranic.
Example: reciting al-Fatiha as dua is allowed; reciting Surah al-Masad cannot be turned into dua because its meaning is not supplication.
3
Explore the teaching method for a menstruating teacher
A female Quran teacher who menstruates may continue teaching using specific methods: word-by-word (al-Karhi) or half-verse (al-Tahawi). This shows that teaching with pauses avoids reciting continuous Quran.
Both methods are acceptable; the Tahawi method is preferred by some.
4
Analyze the qunut dispute
The passage addresses whether the qunut supplication in witr is considered part of the Quran. The Hanafi position is that it is not, so a junub may recite it as dua without prohibition.
The dispute stems from a narration from Ibn Mas'ud, but the majority of Companions reject it.
5
Apply the prohibition of touching the mushaf
The verse 'none touch it except the purified' and the hadith are cited to prohibit touching the mushaf by those in major impurity. A person with minor impurity may touch with a barrier or by using a tool.
This is a separate prohibition from recitation.

Whoever wishes to see details of its chain should refer to al-Sa'ayah.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding rulings on the interaction of menstruating women and junub with the Quran. The author begins by explaining that if a woman in menstruation says words that are Quranic but with an intention of thanks or supplication (not recitation), it is permissible. The key principle is that intention can change the legal nature of the words from Quranic to non-Quranic. For example, reciting al-Fatiha as a dua is allowed, but reciting Surah al-Masad (Abu Lahab) cannot be turned into non-Quranic because its meaning is not supplication. Next, the author addresses a female teacher of the Quran who menstruates. There are two views: according to al-Karhi, she may teach word by word with pauses; according to al-Tahawi, she may teach half a verse and then pause. The issue of qunut supplication is discussed: some early scholars considered qunut to be part of the Quran, so reciting it by a junub would be prohibited; but the relied-upon Hanafi position is that it is not from the Quran, so it is not disliked. The passage then moves to other supplications (duas) and remembrances (adhkar), stating they are permissible for junub and menstruating women based on the hadith that the Prophet remembered Allah in all states. Reading the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms is disliked (makruh) because, although they are divine scriptures, their recitation is not part of our ritual acts. The passage clarifies the distinction between major impurity (janabah, menstruation, post-natal bleeding) and minor impurity (hadath): a person with minor impurity may recite Quran by heart or touch the mushaf with a barrier or with the help of a pure person. Finally, it states the prohibition of touching the Quran for those in major impurity, citing the Quranic verse (56:79) and the hadith 'do not touch the Quran except in a state of purity.' The overall ruling is that menstruating women and junub may not recite Quran with the intention of tilawah, nor touch the mushaf, but they may engage in dhikr and dua.

Key Terms
الحائض (al-ha'id): A woman who is menstruating, i.e., experiencing the monthly blood flow.
الجنب (al-junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity due to sexual intercourse or seminal emission, requiring ghusl.
المحدث (al-muhdith): A person with minor ritual impurity (i.e., without wudu), as opposed to major impurity.
القنوت (al-qunut): A supplication recited in the witr prayer, especially in the Hanafi school.
التلاوة (al-tilawah): The act of reciting the Quran with the intention of worshipful recitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, is a junub allowed to recite the qunut supplication during witr prayer?
A) Yes, because it is not Quran, and supplications are permissible.
B) No, because it is considered to be part of the Quran.
C) Yes, only if he has wudu.
D) No, because it is a supplication and only Quran recitation is prohibited.

Key Takeaway
A menstruating woman or junub may not recite Quran with the intention of tilawah nor touch the mushaf, but their intention can transform Quranic words into permissible supplication.

Touching and Writing the Quran in a State of Janabah
Commentary on matn statements about sleeve touch and dirham with Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on a junub touching the Quran with a barrier, handling a dirham with Quranic text, and writing the Quran on a separate surface.

Core Idea
A barrier that is a follower of the body (sleeve) still incurs prohibition; a separate barrier (purse) removes it.

This lesson examines three practical scenarios for a person in a state of major impurity (junub): touching the Quran with a sleeve, carrying a coin engraved with Quranic verses, and writing Quranic text on paper placed on a board. The Hanafi school has detailed rulings based on whether the barrier is considered a 'follower' of the body or a separate covering. We will trace the reasoning and internal disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each scenario (sleeve touch, dirham in purse, writing on board), determine the act and the hukm level (makruh, haram, permissible, etc.) as stated in the matn.
2
Locate the dalil or basis
For the sleeve touch, the proof is the principle that the sleeve is a 'follower' of the body; for the dirham, the analogy to a mushaf; for writing, the qiyas between partial and full Quran.
Each ruling is supported by either explicit text or qiyas.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding writing on a separate board. Identify each imam's reasoning and the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
This is an internal Hanafi difference, not between madhahib.
4
Analyze the tarjih
For the sleeve touch, the commentary indicates that the corrected view (in al-Hidayah) is makruh tahrimi, though many scholars disagree. Identify which view is mufta bihi.
The tarjih is based on the authority of al-Hidayah and claims of zahir al-riwaya.
5
Apply the rulings to a new case
Given a new scenario (e.g., a junub wearing gloves touches the Quran), determine the ruling using the principles learned.
Use the distinction between 'follower' barrier and separate barrier.

[3] His statement: 'except in a purse'; with damm on the sad and tashdid on the ra', in Persian: 'hamyani' and 'kise', meaning if there is a dirham on which is written some amount of Quran, it is not permissible for a junub to touch it because its ruling is the ruling of the Quran, except if it is in a purse, for it is like a separate cover. (4) His statement: 'he intended...' etc.; the purpose is to rebut what might be understood from the apparent wording of the matn that the prohibition is only for touching what has a surah written on it, not what has less than a surah, because the mention of 'surah' occurs customarily according to habit, otherwise the ruling is general for every amount.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn from the book of purification, specifically addressing rulings related to a junub (person in major impurity) interacting with the Quran. The matn states: 'Touching (the Quran) with the sleeve is disliked, and there is no dirham with a surah (written on it) except in a purse.' The commentary expands on three sub-issues:

First, regarding writing the Quran: If a junub wants to write Quranic text on paper placed on a separate board such that he does not directly touch the written letters, Abu Yusuf permits it based on restricting the prohibition to the part actually touched, while Muhammad disallows it because any paper with even part of the Quran shares the same ruling as a full Quran. This is an intra-Hanafi difference based on how broadly the prohibition applies.

Second, touching with the sleeve: The matn says this is 'disliked' (makruh), and the commentary clarifies it is makruh tahriman (prohibitively disliked) in the relied-upon view. However, it notes a difference: some scholars, including those cited in al-Muhit and al-Kafi, say it is not disliked because the prohibition is only for direct skin contact, and the sleeve acts as a barrier. The author sides with the view that it is makruh because the sleeve is a follower of the body (tabi'), but the majority hold the contrary.

Third, a dirham with Quranic writing: The matn says 'no dirham with a surah except in a purse.' The commentary explains that the ruling applies to any amount of Quran, not just a full surah; the mention of 'surah' is due to custom. The dirham is treated like a copy of the Quran, so a junub cannot touch it directly, but if it is in a separate purse, the purse acts as a barrier, making it permissible.

Key Terms
الكم (al-kumm): The sleeve of a garment, which touches the Quran as a barrier.
صرة (surrah): A purse or pouch containing the dirham, serving as a separate barrier.
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity (janabah), who is prohibited from touching the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for a junub writing the Quran on paper placed on a separate board such that he does not touch the writing?
A) It is absolutely prohibited according to all Hanafi imams.
B) It is permissible according to Abu Yusuf but prohibited according to Muhammad.
C) It is permissible according to both if the board is wood.
D) It is prohibited only if the entire surah is written.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is between barriers that are followers of the body (sleeve) and separate barriers (purse); the former incurs karahah, the latter removes prohibition.

Intercourse After Cessation of Bleeding: The Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on when intercourse becomes permissible after bleeding stops before ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intercourse after the cessation of menses or lochia and explain the condition of the passage of one prayer time without ghusl.

Core Idea
Intercourse is permissible without ghusl after bleeding stops, but only after the maximum period or the passage of a prayer time.

When a woman's menstrual or postpartum bleeding stops, the default ruling is that she cannot have intercourse until she purifies herself with ghusl. However, Hanafi fiqh provides a nuanced rule: if the bleeding stops after the maximum usual duration (10 days for menses, 40 for lochia), intercourse is allowed even before ghusl. If it stops before the maximum, intercourse is only permissible after enough time has passed to perform ghusl and begin a prayer—at which point she is legally considered pure, even if she hasn't bathed. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between bleeding that stops after the maximum duration (10 days for menses, 40 for lochia) and before it. The ruling differs in each case.
If bleeding stops at exactly the maximum, it counts as 'after'.
2
Ruling for 'after maximum'
Intercourse is permissible immediately, even without ghusl, because the bleeding is now considered istihadah (irregular bleeding), not menstruation.
The woman is legally pure from the moment of cessation.
3
Ruling for 'before maximum' - condition
Intercourse is not allowed until the passage of the minimum time needed to perform ghusl and the opening takbir (takbirat al-ihram) from the end of a prayer time. This time is about the duration between Dhuhr and Asr.
The passage of this time makes her pure in ruling (hukman), even if she hasn't bathed.
4
Understand the legal reasoning (dalil)
When the prayer becomes a debt (dayn) upon her—meaning she is obligated to make it up—it indicates that she is legally pure, because obligation presupposes purity. This is the key legal fiction.
The prayer is considered due at the end of its time; if she could have performed ghusl and prayed but didn't, the prayer becomes a debt.
5
Apply to a concrete scenario
Take a woman whose menses stops on the 8th day (less than 10). If it stops at the beginning of Dhuhr, she must wait until the time of Asr begins (i.e., the end of Dhuhr time). At that point, even without ghusl, intercourse is permissible.
She remains prohibited from reading Quran until she performs ghusl, as she is still like a junub in that regard.
